# Whatâ€™s the Difference Between a â€œFuruâ€ and an Analyst? (with book recommendations)

> Let me explain... (free post, off-topic)

**Date:** 2025-01-29T22:11:46.429Z
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/whats-the-difference-between-a-furu

---

Whatâ€™s the Difference Between a â€œFuruâ€ and an Analyst? (with book recommendations)Let me explain... (free post, off-topic)May 03, 20255822ShareThis is an important topic, because fraud has always followed in historyâ€™s footstepsâ€”scams that preyed on the uninformed or impulsive, leaving them financially ruined. With the spread of the telephone, TV, and then the internet, these schemes have grown even more brazen. Although the law tries to regulate them, it canâ€™t truly protect victimsâ€”ultimately each person decides whether to hand over their money, especially if they havenâ€™t done their due diligence to check whether the promisorâ€™s claim is legit.The essence of liberal capitalism is that the consumer becomes a product, complete with all their personal data and ignorance. Companies, banks, brokers, and businesses trample over one another in fierce competition to get you to spend your hard-earned money with them. This is especially true in the West, where consumer-protection regulations are disastrously lax. And thereâ€™s exactly one reason and one goal behind it all: GDP.Laypeople tend to gravitate toward stock-market â€œgurusâ€ (furus) because their statements are straightforward. Even if theyâ€™re not always right, â€œat least they say something.â€ In contrast, an analyst, in the eyes of a novice, is someone who rambles about complex charts and dynamics without making a clear, straight-ahead market-direction prediction. So the average person picks the guru, simply because someone at least tells them what to do. They donâ€™t have to thinkâ€”just copy-trade, and if they lose, they can blame it on the guru (though there are no real consequences). You donâ€™t get that with an analyst.Stochastic Volatility - Options market insights is a reader-supported publication. To receive new posts and support my work, consider becoming a free or paid subscriber.SubscribeFURUSBecause furus live off non-negligible subscription fees, they need to strive to be as accurate as possible. They pay for analyses and even stealâ€¦ I mean borrow information from other gurus, since competition in this â€œindustryâ€ is fierce. Their hit rates therefore vary.But their content tends to be overly straightforward. Why? Because furus are in a guessing race with each other. A furu must demonstrate a certain hit rate to appear credible to subscribersâ€”so they canâ€™t make ambiguous, polysemous statements. And if theyâ€™re wrong, the subscriberâ€™s risk management techniques are blamed (though teaching risk management is something furus rarely do). Furus lie about their winâ€“loss stats, claiming special knowledge that makes them able to predict every future market moves. They promise this secret knowledge with massive overnight profits and laugh at Warren Buffett.The key attributeâ€¦Okay, this is all very common among anyone who are actively marketing themselves. But the key attribute is the lie that sets furus apart from analysts is the claim that market movements are always precisely predictable. They say this because, if market moves were always predictable, their â€œsignal serviceâ€ would be justifiedâ€”and if a prediction hits, they can take full credit. That, however, is simply wrong.ANALYSTSA responsible analyst doesnâ€™t think that way. You all know already that probability measures the area of specific event-sets within the universe of possible outcomes. There are times when certain market moves have an estimated probability above 80â€“90%. But a responsible analyst always thinks in terms of a probability distribution. That means examining the driving engine behind current market dynamics, then mapping out possible scenarios and their conditions (events, timing, price levels, and their implications). From this you get scenarios A, B, C, and D, each with assigned probabilities. Once you understand the market-dynamic engine, the conditions for A, B, C, and D can be well-defined. A subscribing customer can then apply the analystâ€™s framework to shape their own portfolio, trading style, and strategy. A responsible analyst must remain unbiased about market direction, since only one move has a 99.99â€¦% probabilityâ€”namely, that price will move from left to right. Even that isnâ€™t 100%, since global catastrophes have non-zero probability. After mapping the dynamics and defining scenarios and their conditions, the analyst could design a portfolio strategyâ€”but that falls under personalized financial or investment advice, which is a legal category unto itself. Iâ€™ll note that even a guruâ€™s â€œsignal serviceâ€ borders on that line and often falls into illegality.So, unlike gurus who pander to lazy mindsets, an analystâ€™s article is merely a foundation. The trader uses their own knowledge to build their trading, investing, and risk-management strategies. From that point on, itâ€™s their responsibility. This is crucial to understand this distinction.Unnecessary disclaimerâ€¦As I clarified at the start, my Substack service is focused on providing analysis only. It does not include signal services or personalized (or even general) financial or investment advice. Its primary goal is to satisfy the year-long demand for insight into how I think about marketsâ€” that I try to do through daily and weekly analyses, as well as themed educational posts.The key is learning to think in distributions. If you bet according to a well-estimated distribution of probabilities, your winning position will offset losses on the other side. Once you see the winning-condition scenario unfolding, you close your losing positions and increase your winners. This is how the pros do it. But it truly requires work.Thereâ€™s a wealth of excellent educational material on the subject freely available online. Here are just a few to get you started (beside every free articles I attached to my educational posts):Richard D. Wyckoff - The Day Traderâ€™s BibleBenjamin Graham - The Intelligent Investor G. C. Selden - Psychology of the Stock MarketJ. J. Butler - Successful Stock Speculation Sheldon Natenberg - Option Volatility & Pricing Collin Bennett - Trading Volatility Nassim Taleb - Dynamics HedgingAnd also I highly recommend these following books for speculants: CÄƒtÄƒlin BÄƒrboianu - Probability Guide to GamblingAn exhaustive compendium of probability results for dice, roulette, slots, poker, and sports bets, with rigorous finite-sample probability foundations and combinatorial analysis. and - Understanding Your Game: A Mathematician's Advice for Rational and Safe GamblingEdward Packel - The Mathematics of Games and Gambling A mathematically sophisticatedâ€”but accessibleâ€”text covering game-theoretic models and probabilistic analysis for a broad array of gambling scenarios, including sports betting.Edward O. Thorp - The Mathematics of GamblingBy the pioneer of card-counting and quantitative trading; explains probability calculus in blackjack, baccarat, and introduces risk management principles through precise mathematical modelingDavid L. Dickinson - Gambling Habits and Probability Judgements in a Bayesian Task EnvironmentExamines how gamblers update beliefs using Bayesâ€™ theorem, shedding light on cognitive biases in probability assessments. Siam Malhotra - Statistical Models and Predictive Algorithms in Sports Betting Bayesian Approaches, Machine Learning, and Monte Carlo SimulationsSurveys Bayesian methods, machine-learning techniques, and Monte Carlo simulations for modeling sports outcomes, emphasizing rigorous probabilistic frameworks. Walsh & Joshi - Machine Learning for Sports Betting: Should Model Selection Be Based on Accuracy or Calibration?Demonstrates that selecting predictive models on calibration (i.e., probabilities that match observed frequencies) rather than classification accuracy yields superior expected returns. Karl Whelan - Risk Aversion and Favouriteâ€“Longshot Bias in a Competitive Fixed-Odds Betting MarketEmpirical and theoretical analysis of why longshot bets underperformâ€”and how risk preferences shape market oddsâ€”in online fixed-odds platforms.There is no royal roadâ€¦This is just a small taste of the freely available studies and books you can learn fromâ€”not to mention the articles Iâ€™ve linked in my educational posts. As you can see, peopleâ€™s problem isnâ€™t a lack of sources but plain laziness. As Warren Buffett said, â€œThe more you learn, the more you earn.â€And my favorite comes from Dwayne Johnson: â€œPeople ask me whatâ€™s the key to success? The key is there is no key. Be humble, be hungry, and always be the hardest worker in the room.â€Words to live byâ€¦Stochastic Volatility - Options market insights is a reader-supported publication. # Window of Risk Pt. 2 - Flows update 

> What on earth is going on in these markets?

**Date:** 2025-01-29T22:11:46.429Z
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/window-of-risk-pt-2-flows-update

---

Market analysisWindow of Risk Pt. 2 - Flows update Oct 29, 20253042ShareWhat on earth is going on in these markets?There are no gap fills, valuation metrics are on the moon, the put/call ratio is in Chinaâ€”along with market breadthâ€”while the SPX/VIX/VVIX correlation is trending higher. Every dip is being bought, levered up via the options market; short-vol and short-vomma trades are aggressively crowded, and lately theyâ€™ve even been attacking realised volatility and realised vol-of-vol too.The reasons should be sought in the globally shifting geopolitical landscapeâ€”much like when political parties campaign for power: burnishing their achievements by falsifying and wrenching them out of context to look better to their supporters. I wrote at length about this earlier, in April. Please (re)read it. Pro tip: donâ€™t believe that China will ever give up. Eastern people are patient and tactical, unlike Trumpâ€¦Window of RiskMy earlier â€œWindow of Riskâ€ (WoR) calls this year were: Februaryâ€“April window â€” I flagged the April drop days in advance, with then-unbelievable downside targets that were hit.Correction in May â€” My primary WoR ran from the May ECB rate decision to mid-May (Trumpâ€™s visit to the Middle East). We saw a ~97-point decline in SPX, but the market did not break my target level from which momentum could have flipped into left-tail risk.(Later, a 124-point decline materialized after Trumpâ€™s visit, but that wasnâ€™t the risk I expected; it followed directly from weekly positioning with an exact target. The same applies to the 107-point decline in the 16â€“20 June week.)July/August window â€” Essentially my original major WoR: the post-NFP and FOMC period, once tariff effects began to show up in the data. I marked it into mid-August. The effects did start to appear, and we got a 215-point declineâ€”still not a break of the level that would have triggered the left-tail risk Iâ€™d been flagging.(In mid-August another 138-point decline followed, but again the structure held. By mid-July it had also become clear to me that the market would remain supported, and I emphasised that my WoR was shifting towards the post-September OpEx period.)Post-September OpEx â€” I called for supportive flows into Monday and the WoR starting on Tuesday: that produced a 130.3-point pullback. I then called for volatility and vomma supply, and for the week of 6 October I flagged weakness and a potential vol spike into the Thuâ€“Fri window, which delivered a 213.8-point pullback.The common feature of these shake-offs is that none of them broke the structureâ€”neither in dynamics nor at key levelsâ€”and this was highly likely on the basis of weekly flow analysis, as I kept updating in intraday posts. Quantitatively speaking, even the quant funds did not hit any triggers. As I warned, risk kept being eroded via time correctionâ€”a superficial, paper-only erosion rather than a real one. SubscribeFlowsSPX/VIX/VVIX are green â€” though the last only slightly, relatively speaking. This says that whole curve is bid. Yet, there is still no volatility squeeze, because realised volatility and realised vol-of-vol remain managed intraday and via weekly zomma. What I mean is this: to the downside, realised volatility is being sold, as the downside convexity of the short-speed profile compresses intraday returns; to the upside, returns can widen because there is an upward vacuum. On an intraday basis, both realised and implied volatility are being sold via tail gamma and VIX. Zomma is also being supplied again. Meaning, we are at the right-end of the curve, where the gamma/IV relationship is positive, managing realised vol in the event of a gamma squeeze. Because being short gamma means to be short realized volatility.These are the force that is preventing any volatility squeeze for now. And this is crucial for the insiders because volatility would indicate crash now, something they cannot let to happen again. SubscribeRetail speculators have emerged as a major force (over 22% of total volume), consistently buying stocks even as institutions step back roughly 85% of the time. 0DTE flows made up about 62.4% of total SPX options volume. Meanwhile, large institutions are keeping their core holdings (so as not to lag the indices) but have added hedges and simultaneously reduced positions in roughly 90% of the weeks since August. They are sceptical of this rally; their sentiment is heavily negative, they see greater risk in holding longs, and they are scaling out into strength â€” but only gradually, so as not to bombard the market with sell orders or risk underperformance. They want to reload once the market is trading for discount again.The supply is being absorbed by dealersâ€™ intraday and weekly longer realised- and implied-volatility positions on the downside, and by retail flow. And this is crucial, because it means the momentum we are experiencing is not fully organic. The constant, shady misses of gap fills are a tell. In other words, this rally is not the result of large long-term institutions pouring in; it is largely mechanical, driven by dealers and fast-money traders leveraging options. The exact mechanisms of these dynamics are described in-depth in this (free) September post: Window of Risk, Disbalance - Investigating the story that the market tellsAlmaÂ·September 18, 2025Read full storyOctober rate decision FOMCSPX/yields correlation is positive ahead of the FOMC, though it has eased over the past few sessions. That may suggest Bessent is not expecting an especially dovish aftermath, but only short-lived, we will see.  A rate cut is widely viewed as a done deal and largely priced in, which is why forward guidance matters much more at this point. If the Fed does not exceed dovish expectations, there is a riskâ€”indeed, a short-term opportunityâ€”of a â€œsell-the-newsâ€ dip. Markets also expect the Fed to announce an end to quantitative tightening. I do not expect any crash to happen in the mid term. However, I still expect my 6â€“10% correction to play out soon. Statistically, when the Fed cut rates when the market was within 1â€“3% of an all-time high, the market was up by 14.95% on average within the next 12 months, in an average of 96.5% of cases (including 2019 and 2024). Novemberâ€“December window is historically the strongest months for actual buyback executions and volatility selling.However, keep in mind that going against seasonality can be a major source of liquidity for the insiders if they want to reloadâ€”not fully going against it, but enough to create a brief scare before the end-of-year rally. Corporate demand is set to return robustly in November, adding a steady bid for stocks. ShutdownThe shutdown was not accounted for when I wrote my â€˜Window of Riskâ€™ post in September. I marked the end of that window at the end of the buyback blackout. That is right, butâ€”as I keep emphasisingâ€”on the longer tenor there is a synthetic, hidden long vomma in the market due to geopolitical risk, which makes the market headline-sensitive. So the â€˜Window of Riskâ€™ will end when shutdown ends and we start to receive the data again.Active data collection is halted under the Labor Departmentâ€™s contingency plan. The September CPI was compiled and released late (24 October) because much of that monthâ€™s pricing had already been gathered; October CPI collection has been disrupted, and the White House and media are signalling no October CPI release next month. This means survey interviewing, price collection, data processing and most publications stop. We can therefore expect heavier revisions later and, in some cases, skipped months, and the data may become more controlled. When the funding bill is signed (a continuing resolution or full-year appropriations), the shutdown ends. No date yet.Target and strategyAs you may know, Iâ€™m net bullish for the next 4-5 years. I already covered this.My recent target for this window is shown here: As you can see on the chart, I estimate the maximum target for a potential melt-up at 7,146.88 for SPX and a downside target at the August-peak/October-bottom liquidity zone.This is a primary target, because the book has been very thin since the summer. Given this thin positioning data, it is hard to estimate exact potential scenarios.However, I donâ€™t expect any crashâ€”only a pullback, a local left-tail event where we can take yearly risk-on bets. Until structure breaks and pullback is confirmed I use flies, ratios and calendar spreads as a hedge to catch front-end volatility. Iâ€™ll keep you updated in the Sunday posts and intraday posts with levels, dynamics and distribution. Stochastic Volatility - Options market insights is a reader-supported publication. # A Guide to Reading My Daily Posts

> Every time you look at tools such as put/call deltas, ratios, or even monitor changes in order flow (e.g., the ratio between selling and buying volume), you get lagging data.

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/a-guide-to-reading-my-daily-posts

---

Educational postsA Guide to Reading My Daily PostsNov 11, 2025âˆ™ Paid4865ShareEvery time you look at tools such as put/call deltas, ratios, or even monitor changes in order flow (e.g., the ratio between selling and buying volume), you get lagging data.By contrast, my method is to reverse-engineer the structure the market is pricing in, decode the hidden patterns (short or long butterfly spreads or risk-reversal patterns), and derive both the key pivot ranges from expected flows and the implied distribution that the market is pricing in. As Iâ€™ve already written in my earlier educational posts about trading strategies, the pivot and the probability levels primarily function as checkpoints that provide information about the given price action and the underlying sentiment. I mark downside and upside targets, as well as local pinning and additional supportive/suppressive targets, separately. In regards of the weekly post, this is a discrepancy of what the market expected for the week. This is where the edge lies, because:1) If the market behaves as it is expected, then it is an easy play, sentiment remains in check, volatility remains controlled, even if the expectations are negative;2) If the market moves more above the expectations, then the sentiment is out of control (more bearish/bullish), reinforcing the trend;3) If the market divergences from the initial expectations, thats the strongest signal, because that also means that the initial positioning must be shifted providing mechanical support too. However, the trend must be confirmed!Now, Friday will be the tell if the GDP reaction was an actual shift in sentiment (even if forced) or just the temporal product of IC and mechanical flows solely on a thin tape.SubscribeAfter all these, Trump posted: This post felt literally just like if he would have answered to my last two posts I dropped during the weekendâ€¦There are some small fibs about trade deficit, investments and inflation, but the core message aligns with my â€œBessent putâ€ thesis I just wrote about few days ago, that they are about to reshape the reaction function to the economy. Trump claims that in good old days, markets were rallied on good news. Depends on what he meant by â€œold daysâ€: after the dotcom bubble, it was net true until 2020, however before 90s it was exactly the opposite. As long as core inflation remains above target, market doesnâ€™t like â€˜good newsâ€™â€¦ ot it should not doâ€¦However what Trump promises by this post is that they will rig the game even more fundamentaly in order to keep the markets up. UK and US both did something similar in the 70s, and also Turkey did so two years ago driving their economy into a hyperinflation. All of these ended up in forced tightenings after.Whatâ€™s the purpose? Well, if you get GDP up/prices up and policymakers still try to push easier conditions, the best case is a â€œfinancial repressionâ€ with lower real rates that erode the real burden of debt, but at the cost of higher inflation volatility and distortions.However there is a risk that long-end yields still rise, and so debt service can worsen. And this is were the Bessent-put comes into playâ€¦They expand long-end buyback operations and aim to keep vol low. But this will keep inflation sticky again. This tells me that Trump wants to bring back the post-WW2 â€œyield curve controlâ€ scheme later.The Fed implemented a form of yield-curve targeting, capping rates at multiple maturities (famously ~2.5% on long bonds). This made mortgages, corporate loans, and other investments cheaper, stimulating the economy.The market rallied like ~135% from 1942 to 1951 March, when they unwound this regime. Because once inflation pressures rise, holding down long yields can require balance-sheet expansion and/or regulation-like controls, which can undermine credibility and create an eventual break. Now, you can see a three-years long bearmarket started in 1946 May.This was because of sharp reflation after price controls were eliminated. As a result of YCC, bill yields pegged at 0.375% and the long bond capped at 2.5%, with the Fed having to buy securities to defend the structure if markets didnâ€™t want to hold them at those yields. Now, even if nominal yields were anchored, real yields could become sharply negative during the inflation spike, creating a noisy valuation environment.Later, as inflation expectations persisted, Fed went for tightening in 1948, and the economy entered its first postwar recession in Nov of 1948, and then the cycle reset. We have however this AI-boom too, that is supportive only if the developments deliver real productivity, shifting the environment towards positive supply-side shock. But the risk is high that the AI is demand today but wonâ€™t become a supply tomorrow. Debt/GDP could look better in nominal terms, but productivity would be lower and capital allocation would degrade. And this would produce a very similar or â€œrhymingâ€ pattern of what happened in the last century. # Central Banks, Tariffs, Market (Weekly post 07-11/April)

> Before we dive inâ€¦

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/central-banks-tariffs-market-weekly

---

Market analysisCentral Banks, Tariffs, Market (Weekly post 07-11/April)Apr 06, 2025âˆ™ Paid4366ShareBefore we dive inâ€¦Geopolitical context,Tariff war, World re-polarization, from off-shore to friend-shore,Emerging deflationary concerns on the horizont.CDS Index: Gold Futures: I canâ€™t stress enough that the market not only hasnâ€™t come out of the woods yet, it has only just entered it.We reached a bottom here, and weâ€™ll see vol selling to brake that car on the icy road. In this environment, any not-bad news is good news. But the underlying trend is still bearish until fiscal and negotiation outcomes change the picture.Trump wants to push through tax cuts and a debt ceiling increase; if they fail, the U.S. is likely to plunge into a quick recession. But the elite and the banks and funds have every tool to bail the economy out. As I keep saying, the real reason behind the tariffs is that Trumpâ€™s strategy is to force the EU and other affected nations to the negotiation table. Some countries have already started negotiating. And with Klaus Schwab stepping aside, there may be an opportunity for a reformed approachâ€”one that might be ready to engage with the U.S. on more favorable terms, as part of a broader realignment that acknowledges the limits of pure globalization.(Schwab was a long-time symbol of the globalist eliteâ€”the network of business and policy leaders who traditionally guided global economic policy and multilateral cooperation. With this move, the globalist elite, recognizing the need to adapt to a new reality, might be more willing to consider U.S. proposals that align with the current economic and geopolitical landscape.)The current situation requires a lot of patience, because these events all hinge on the decisions of leaders. Predicting them in advance isnâ€™t worthwhileâ€”it can create unnecessary bias. Here, we must watch, wait, and adapt.This is what Powell said on Friday. Doesnâ€™t it sounds familiar? (hint: my premarket post)And what did Trump say? Letâ€™s break it downâ€¦The new tit-for-tat â€œtariff warâ€ initiated by the U.S. is reshuffling the global trade deck. Letâ€™s first identify which countries and sectors are most exposed to the U.S. trade balance and thus in the crosshairs of tariff policy, and how they are responding.China is by far the largest source of the U.S. trade deficit (goods deficit with China was about $350-400 billion per year pre-tariffs), China is the prime target. The U.S. tariffs (now averaging 34% on Chinese good) hit electronics, machinery, furniture, and other consumer products. Chinaâ€™s exposure to U.S. demand is significant â€“ around 18% of Chinaâ€™s goods exports went to the U.S. pre-tariff. The immediate impact is disruption of supply chains. Multinational companies with factories in China (toys, apparel, consumer electronics) are scrambling to reroute orders to other Asian countries or domestically in the U.S./Mexico. As a result, beneficiaries include Vietnam, Mexico, and other Southeast Asian nations which can supply similar goods. Indeed, even before this escalation, Vietnam and India saw rising U.S. imports as firms â€œChina+1â€ strategy took hold. Now that will accelerate. However, as CSIS notes, the scale and scope of Trumpâ€™s new tariffs exceeded expectations â€“ he didnâ€™t stop at China. NAFTA (now USMCA) partners traditionally enjoy tariff-free access to the U.S. market. But Trump invoked a â€œreciprocal tariffâ€ doctrine even on allies â€“ threatening 25% tariffs on Canada and Mexico for any sectors where they impose barriers. Specifically, he complained about Canadaâ€™s dairy protections and Mexicoâ€™s auto rules. This is politically sensitive because USMCA is in force. Mexican officials have warned that U.S. tariffs violate the agreement and have prepared retaliatory tariffs on U.S. corn and machinery if enacted. Mexico is highly exposed: itâ€™s the U.S.â€™s second-largest trading partner, with a deficit of around $130B. Sectors at stake: autos and auto parts (huge cross-border industry), electronics, appliances, and agriculture (Mexico imports a lot of U.S. grain, and exports vegetables and beer). So far, Trump gave a short reprieve to USMCA partners to negotiate. The hope is heâ€™s using the threat as leverage to force, say, more U.S. content in autos or a sunset clause renegotiation. If he actually slapped tariffs, it would be economically damaging to border states and U.S. companies integrated with Mexico. Thus, we may see intense negotiations where Mexico perhaps agrees to quotas or voluntary export restraints in some sectors to appease Trump. Canada, too, may make concessions (e.g. further opening its dairy market, as it partially did in USMCA). The â€œcoalition of the willingâ€ in trade might ironically be U.S. neighbors trying to appease U.S. demands. Canadian and Mexican leaders are also coordinating with the EU and Asia to form a united front at the WTO, but the WTOâ€™s Appellate Body is defunct (partly due to U.S. blocking), limiting legal recourse.The EU as a whole runs a trade surplus with the U.S. (~$200B in goods, partly offset by a U.S. surplus in services). Germany is a major contributor via cars and machinery. The new U.S. tariffs hit European autos with a 10% duty (since the EU has a 10% tariff on U.S. cars, Trump mirrored it â€“ â€œreciprocalâ€) and steel/aluminum with 10% (again citing existing EU tariffs). The EU swiftly announced it would â€œgive as good as it getsâ€ with proportionate tariffs. Brussels targeted iconic American exports like Harley-Davidson motorcycles, Kentucky bourbon, and even iPhones (the $2,300 iPhone quip â€“ thatâ€™s an estimate of future cost if all tariffs fully applied). Europeâ€™s strategy is twofold: retaliate to pressure the U.S. politically, but also seek dialogue. EU Trade Commissioner has flown to Washington to propose a plurilateral forum on tariff reduction, trying to coax the U.S. back to a negotiated solution. Both Japan and South Korea have large trade surpluses with the U.S. in automobiles, electronics, etc. Japanâ€™s surplus (~$70B) has long irked Trump. He revived talk of a 25% auto tariff, which would hit Toyota, Honda, etc. Japan is extremely exposed in autos â€“ millions of cars exported. However, Japanâ€™s strategy is to emphasize its investments in U.S. factories (many Japanese carmakers build in America, employing tens of thousands). South Korea renegotiated KORUS FTA with Trump in 2018 to avoid tariffs by limiting steel exports and opening auto quotas. Theyâ€™ll likely abide by those terms and hope to dodge new tariffs. Both Japan and Korea are also key players in semiconductor and battery supply chains â€“ ironically, sectors the U.S. wants to secure. So tariffs there could be self-defeating. We might see sectoral exemptions â€“ e.g. rare earths, EV batteries, etc., might be spared due to strategic importance.Vietnam, Taiwan, Thailand and Malaysia have seen surging exports to the U.S., partly taking share from China. Vietnam now has a >$100B surplus with the U.S., making it potentially the next target. The U.S. has already labeled Vietnam a currency manipulator in the past. However, Vietnam is a linchpin of U.S. Indo-Pacific strategy â€“ itâ€™s a communist country but leans toward the U.S. to counter China.  The pattern could be: hammer China publicly, quietly negotiate with Vietnam/others to not let them become too big of conduits for Chinese goods. Countries like Vietnam and Malaysia will attempt to comply with rules of origin, to show they arenâ€™t just repackaging Chinese products. If they succeed, they gain as trade diverts to them. If the U.S. also targets them, then the whole global trade pie shrinks substantially (a true Smoot-Hawley scenario).Some countries run deficits with the U.S. (meaning U.S. has surplus). They include UK, Netherlands (due to services and trans-shipments), and some in Latin America. Those countries are less exposed to tariffs, but more exposed to slowdown in U.S. demand. For example, if U.S. consumers pay more for tariffs, they may import less from everywhere, not just tariffed countries. So even nations not directly hit by tariffs might suffer collateral export declines.We also see a quickening of the â€œreconfiguration of supply chainsâ€ that began with Covid-19 and U.S.-China economic cold war tensions. Thereâ€™s a push for â€œfriend-shoringâ€ â€“ sourcing from countries deemed friendly to the U.S. (like Mexico, or maybe India). Southeast Asia stands to benefit: Thailand might get more electronics assembly, Indonesia more furniture/textile orders, etc. U.S. domestic manufacturing could see a boost in certain sectors (steel, perhaps semiconductors with CHIPS Act support, etc.), but capacity constraints and higher costs mean it canâ€™t replace Asia overnight.One sector to watch is agriculture. Chinaâ€™s retaliation so far focused on manufactured goods tariffs, but if it extends to U.S. agriculture (like soybeans as in 2018), it could hurt U.S. farmers and cause trade diversion to Brazil/Argentina for soy, or Australia for grains. On April 4, China did announce tariffs on all U.S. goods including agri, which implies U.S. farmers will indeed face losses as Chinese buyers shift to South America. That puts political pressure on Trump from farm states, which might force some compromise or subsidies to farmers (like the aid given in 2019 trade war). The Fed is a bit behind the ECB in dovish pivot but is moving that way. Powellâ€™s comments acknowledging tariffsâ€™ impact on inflation and growth show a balanced view. The Fedâ€™s reaction function now weights financial conditions more because the banking system had a scare in 2023 (Silicon Valley Bank and others). The Fed doesnâ€™t want to inadvertently trigger more bank failures or market crashes by overtightening. The tariffs complicate their job: they could add maybe 0.5% to headline inflation later in 2025 (if companies pass costs on), but also subtract perhaps 1% from GDP over 2 years (as global trade shrinks). The bond market is betting the Fed chooses to support growth (cut rates) rather than fight a one-off inflation blip. We can expect the Fed start cutting by Q3 2025. It will also use tools to ensure liquidity â€“ e.g. extending the BTFP (which lends to banks against bonds at par, essentially QE-like support). The Fedâ€™s big concern is un-anchored inflation expectations, but so far long-run inflation expectations have remained around 2-2.5% (helped by the credibility they built). The ECBâ€™s typical reflex (as seen) is early easing on growth fears. The ECB has explicitly said a trade war is a big downside risk and that inflation from tariffs will likely be short-lived, whereas the growth hit could be lasting. That gives them cover to cut rates without worrying they are betraying their inflation mandate â€“ because they expect inflation will actually fall after an initial import cost bump (due to recessionary forces). We saw multiple ECB officials make the case for cuts in March. We anticipate the ECB will announce at least a 25 bp rate cut in May, and possibly signal more to come, reversing course from tightening. Additionally, the ECB might revive some form of asset purchases if credit spreads blow out. There is precedent: in 2019, after trade tensions and weak industry data, Draghi guided rate cuts and restarted QE. This time, Christine Lagarde (ECB President) has warned tariffs â€œunsettle the trade world as we know itâ€ and could have global spillovers. The ECB is coordinating with other central banks â€“ for example, currency swap lines to ensure euro/dollar liquidity. Behaviorally, the ECBâ€™s early hints are meant to shore up confidence (a forward guidance tool) â€“ by saying â€œwe stand ready to actâ€, they hope to prevent a credit crunch in advance. American and European left-wing economic circles argue central banks should not overreact to supply-side inflation at the cost of jobs (they cheered the idea of Fedâ€™s average inflation targeting which tolerates overshoots). They currently would say: â€˜Donâ€™t repeat the errors of the past; the bigger threat is a downturn and financial instability, not a second wave of inflationâ€™. On the right-wing side, especially among some German and U.S. conservatives, there is concern that easing too soon will entrench higher inflation and punish savers, and that central banks might be yielding to political pressure. A dovish ECB would be interpreted as weakness that Trump could exploit.An early ECB cut would support the idea that European nations are willing to compromise for short-term relief. That, in turn, creates a window for Trump to put Europe on the negotiation tableâ€”using the promise of stabilization as leverage for tax cuts or debt ceiling adjustments in the U.S.If Europe shows signs of sticking to high rates despite the trade war rather than easing, then that rigidity can actually worsen market sentiment.Chinaâ€™s response to tariffs is domestic stimulus â€“ the PBoC has cut bank reserve requirement ratios (RRR) and even trimmed policy interest rates to spur lending. It is also guiding banks to keep credit flowing to small exporters hurt by tariffs. The PBoC and Chinaâ€™s state banks may also intervene to steady the yuan if needed, dipping into their $3T reserve war chest. By easing policy, China actually contributes to global liquidity (a more accommodative stance can leak out via increased imports from neighbors, less competition for global capital, etc.). If China were to tighten in the face of tariffs (which it wonâ€™t), that would be pro-cyclical and worsen things. Instead, China is explicitly focusing on stimulus and strengthening ties with alternative trading partners, effectively taking a different route than confrontation â€“ a rational response to mitigate damage. The BoJ is an interesting case. It has been under pressure to normalize policy given that Japan finally saw inflation above 2%. However, global turmoil gives it cover to delay any tightening. In April 2025, the BoJ left its yield curve control in place (10-year JGB yield cap ~1%) and policy rate negative, noting it did not want to contribute to global market stress. Governor Ueda hinted that while domestic conditions might have justified some tightening, the external environment is too risky â€“ essentially a â€œpauseâ€ citing global uncertainty (likely referencing the yen carry unwinds and financial volatility). If the yen surges too much (carry unwind), the BoJ might even intervene (as it did in 2022) to smooth FX moves. But so far, yen strengthening is a natural stabilizer â€“ it tightens Japanâ€™s financial conditions a bit (which the BoJ might quietly welcome to tame import inflation) but not too dramatically yet. The BoJ also provides a stabilizing influence by continuing asset purchases; Japanese investors, faced with domestic caps, will still invest abroad (once volatility settles), providing some support to global markets. In a behavioral sense, the BoJ has been the anchor of low rates â€“ as long as it stays put, some investors will continue to engage in carry trades (perhaps shifting to other funding currencies like euro or Swiss franc if yen is less favorable due to volatility).Trumpâ€™s deliberate use of manufactured chaos â€“ from debt-ceiling brinkmanship to tariff salvos â€“ has created a highly fluid situation. This chaos is a negotiating tactic to keep opponents off-balance and force reactive moves. As weâ€™ve seen, markets and central banks form a reactive feedback loop to these shocks. Trumpâ€™s threats (whether default or trade war) spook markets, markets sell off, then policymakers (e.g. the Fed, ECB) respond to stabilize conditions, which in turn can embolden further aggressive bargaining. Itâ€™s a high-stakes game of chicken.Leading indicators like credit spreads, equity volatility, and PMI surveys become as important to watch as official GDP or CPI releases. If credit spreads start narrowing and equities stabilizing, central banks may feel less urgency to ease aggressively. If, on the other hand, we saw something like a funding strain (LIBOR/OIS spread widening, or a major bank in trouble), policymakers would intervene swiftly (as they did in March 2023 with bank backstops).The German â€œregime shiftâ€ on fiscal policy â€“ overturning decades of austerity â€“ is a game-changer for Europe, and it flew under many investorsâ€™ radar because headlines were dominated by U.S.-China drama. But this unlocks hundreds of billions of Euros for investment. Strategically, this means Europe could become a relative bright spot in global demand late in 2025 and 2026, just as the U.S. perhaps slows under trade pressures. A well-capitalized European defense and infrastructure push can create jobs and profits within Europe. For investors, European equities (particularly industrials, construction, defense companies) may have a more solid fundamental backing now â€“ a structural trend that could outlast the current turmoil. Right-wing European commentators (and some on Wall Street) cheer this as belated but welcome, arguing Europe needs fiscal expansion to share the burden long shouldered by monetary policy. Left-wing voices in Europe also support the investment (especially in green infrastructure), though they urge that it be directed to inclusive growth. From a macro strategy angle, Europeâ€™s fiscal loosening combined with the ECBâ€™s likely easing is a bullish mix for European assets, although partly priced in after the relative outperformance noted in Q1. A risk to monitor is if bond markets question debt sustainability â€“ so far thatâ€™s in check, but if German yields keep rising with big issuance, the ECB may need to tacitly cap yields (possibly via its existing tools like the TPI backstop for Italy).This is a very important precursor of a potential change. SubscribeGeopoliticsI previously highlighted the risk around the Venezuelan situation. By moving to apprehend Maduroâ€”a Russia- and China-aligned dictatorâ€”the U.S. is pushing back against de-dollarization, since the bulk of global oil trade is conducted in dollars, and it can also secure more durable control over global oil supply. That reduces dependence on Middle Eastern OPEC producers and allows oil pricesâ€”and therefore inflationâ€”to be influenced, especially to offset the impact of tariffs.This also contributes to managing the debt burden: the U.S. can borrow cheaply in its own currency because international reserve demand supports the dollar and helps keep U.S. Treasury yields lowerâ€”what Giscard dâ€™Estaing called the â€œexorbitant privilege.â€ In the short term, the market can plausibly expect fiscal support and then monetary support; in the long term, however, this is problematic.China will not ignore this geopolitical realignment, so the geopolitical risk premium remains elevated.Market riskRisk appetite among speculators is above 85%, which is extremely high. SubscribeMicrostructureRetail investors will likely remain important drivers of liquidity and price discovery. This is a hidden short-speed position: we should not expect strong long-squeeze dynamics in a selloff, but it leaves them with more capacity to step in during drawdowns.Rotation is not only occurring at the index level, but also across sectors. Some capital is moving into value and cyclical names. By late 2025, previously lagging segmentsâ€”such as energy and industrialsâ€”remain underrepresented in market cap despite improving fundamentals. If those fundamentals continue to improve, these sectors can gain a larger share of the overall market, and there are already signs that investors are beginning to reallocate in that direction.This temporarily reduces concentration risk, while we can still expect expansion in tech to be framed positively.Overall, supportive forces are still present, but the market needs more room to grow and must keep the bullish narrative alive. I expect more meaningful weakness into spring; until then, the base case is rotation and range-bound trade. This market favors speculators more than investors, and long-term risk remains very high. # CPI, credit spreads, Stagflation, QT + Intraday post (13/May)

> SPX/LTY correlation, Yields, Credit spreads, CPI, Stagflation, Intraday post

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/cpi-credit-spreads-stagflation-qt

---

Market analysisCPI, credit spreads, Stagflation, QT + Intraday post (13/May)SPX/LTY correlation, Yields, Credit spreads, CPI, Stagflation, Intraday postMay 13, 2025âˆ™ Paid2212ShareTrumpâ€™s landed in Saudi. This is a pivotal event. Meanwhile the White House is weighing a deal to export hundreds of thousands of U.S. AI-grade chips to G42, a UAE AI championâ€”an unprecedented pivot in export controls. Signaling that geopolitical lines around technology are fluid, injecting fresh uncertainty into semis and tech valuations. Potentially broadening chip supply could ease tech-hardware inflation pressureâ€”but may provoke pushback if national-security concerns rise. This is what Iâ€™m keep saying, that the stagflationary pressures can be eased, and economy can be bailed out if good deals are done. But the final boss is China. DYNAMICS1) SPX/LTY correlationEquityâ€“yields correlation has jumped to ~70 bps over the past week (from -22% to 52%), signaling that inflation surprises (and hence inflation expectations) have become the primary driver of both yields and equity returns, rather than growth surprises. This abrupt flip resembles the mid-2021 shift documented by the BIS: once inflation is volatile enough, positive correlations persist and diversification benefits fade. Crucial, because the stockâ€“bond correlation depends not on the level of inflation per se but on the relative volatility of growth vs. inflation surprises and their covariance. When inflation uncertainty rises, its shocks loom larger, flipping correlations positive and eroding bondsâ€™ hedge role. With equities and bonds moving together, passive portfolios demand extra compensation for undiversified bond risk, pushing term premiaâ€”and long yieldsâ€”higher over time.CPI expectations remain elevated above the Fedâ€™s 2% goal: April headline is seen at +0.2% m/m (2.4% y/y), core at +0.3% m/m (2.8% y/y), with currentâ€quarter annualized CPI â‰ˆ 3.0%, yearâ€ahead consumer survey at 3.6%, and 5-year breakevens near 2.39%So, basically consensus forecasts expect the April CPI to show inflation was firmer than previously thought, signaling that price pressures remain sticky and complicating the Fedâ€™s calculus for rate cuts.Below-consensus CPI prints will now tend to lower long yields (bond prices up) and boost equitiesâ€”unlike a growth regime, where yields and stocks would diverge. Good inflation news are good for both. While above-consensus inflation prints push yields up (bond prices down) and dent stocks, creating a one-two punch to multi-asset portfolios. Bad inflation news are bad for both.2) U.S. 10-Year Treasury YieldYields fell below 4.00 % in early April (betting on rapid Fed cuts and tariff relief), then surged back above 4.40 % as the Fed held rates and liquidity tightened. The rebound from 4.00 % to 4.40â€“4.50 % shows markets now price a delayed Fed easing cycleâ€”term premia have widened as fixed-income investors demand extra compensation for holding long bonds in a volatile, policy-driven regime. Breakeven inflation (10-year TIPS spread) stood at 2.29 % on May 9, reflecting that markets still expect inflation near the Fedâ€™s 2 % target over the next decade. With longer spiked SPX to LTY correlation, this means that good inflation news (below-expectation CPI) will now lower yields and lift equitiesâ€”as both bond risk premia and equity discount rates ease; while bad inflation news (above-expectation CPI) will raise yields and hurt stocks in tandem, compressing traditional 60/40 diversification benefits.4.30â€“4.50 % likely holds as long as policy and tariff risks remain front-and-center; a sustained move below 4.20 % would require markedly weaker data or fresh Fed dovish signals.Any surprise in inflation prints or a sudden liquidity squeeze (e.g., repo-market stress) could send yields rapidly through the 4.50 % handleâ€”and rip equity valuations.3) CDX High-Yield 5Y SpreadThe credit-default-swap spread on the 5-year high-yield index spiked from ~300 bps in December to a peak near 476 bps in early April (tariff shock/freight-bottle-neck worry), then retraced to ~350 bps today. Itâ€™s above its 100- and 200-day averagesâ€”indicating that, on balance, credit stress remains elevated vs. long-term norms. But itâ€™s below its 50-day average, suggesting a partial cooldown from the spike but not a return to â€œhealthyâ€ levels.A steep curve in CDS spreads (with shorter tenors widening more) can foreshadow mounting default concerns; the high-yield 5-year contractâ€™s stubborn elevation implies that near-term funding strains are priced into credit protections.Spreads north of both long-run averages demonstrate investors demand extra compensation for tail risks in the high-yield universe. If a fresh catalyst (e.g., hawkish Fed remarks or tariff escalations) hits, we will see renewed stress, since dealers and funds remain underhedged in face of a tighter liquidity backdrop. In a good-news regime, weâ€™d expect spreads to compress back toward long-term averages and equity risk-premia to narrow. In a bad-news regime, spreads would re-widen sharply above short-term trends, denting high-yield debt and equity valuations in tandem.So the key is, that the primary concern has shifted away from recession toward managing inflation risks.Lose-lose for FedAs I kept explaining since April Fed datas, Trumpâ€™s tariff policy forced the Fed into a lose-lose situation of choosing between steering the economy into recession or managing a period of stagflation. At its May 7 meeting, Fed officials unanimously decided to keep the benchmark rate unchanged while cautioning that tariffs posed dual threats of higher unemployment and higher inflation. As Powell said that sustained tariff increases â€œare likely to generate a rise in inflation, a slowdown in economic growth, and an increase in unemploymentâ€. Stagflation. Firmer-than-expected CPI readings imply the Fed may hold rates at restrictive levels longer, and any dovish pivot hinges on clear evidence of inflation easing back toward the 2 percent target. Get ready for sharp repricing of both rates and equities, if priceâ€report surprises. VOLATILITY REGIMECoding 1m, 3m realized volatility and vol of vol for SPX: On a monthly basis, RV has fallen down to its 23.36% by May 9, while catched up to 24.90% during yesterday session. However on a three monthly basis, RV is stagnant and grinding up, currently trending at 29.48%The sharp decrease in one monthly RV was simply caused by mathematics, once those four shock days began to roll off a 20-day window (by April 23, T+12; April 24, T+13; etc.), the 1m RV plungedâ€”not because markets calmed, but because the biggest outliers left the sample. A 3m RV has longer memory.Risk-parity, vol-targeting, risk-controlled indices compare the observed RV to a target and scale exposure by min(1, target/RV). Volatility-managed portfolios outperform by de-leveraging in high-vol regimes and increasing leverage when volatility subsides. Until both windows fall below the vol-target threshold, vol-control strategies will remain cautiousâ€”hence minimal fresh buy flows even in the face of a low 1m RV.The real reason is the vol of vol (VoV). Look how much it spikedâ€¦This means that the mean of vol falls while its variance return distribution stays high, signaling fat tails, clustering, and higher sensitivity of risk measures to new shocks, also delta-hedged option returns are systematically negative when exposed to high VoVâ€”investors pay up for the insurance against a volatile volatility regime. (Rough volatility models show that volatility paths are far more jagged than classical diffusionsâ€”implying persistent high VoV even if average vol is low.) There is a lot of mispricing risk here. The takeaway is clear: donâ€™t trust a singleâ€scale vol measure. 1m RV may signal â€œbuyâ€, but VoV and 3m RV still signal â€œsell/neutral.â€ The result: vol-targeters sit on their hands, missing opportunities or being caught off-guard when RV re-spikes.MEAN REVERSIONThe first reversion model, signaling red dot at 5844.18 highs, targeting 5491.06 as the projected â€œcool offâ€ point. 5664.86 is pivot  The model says 67.26% probability for reversion towards 5491.06Size accordingly. The odds are relatively underpricedMay OpEx:Jun OpEx:Based on the VIX at 18.52% as of May 13, 2025, the one-sigma move over the next three trading days (to May 16) is about Â±118 points, corresponding to a 68.2% probability of SPX remaining within that range and 31.8% of ending outside it. The market assigns roughly a 17.3% chance of SPX < 5725 and a 15.7% chance of SPX > 5960 by May 16, as implied by the respective IVs. This aligns with what VIX is showing us, even overestimated by a small 1.2 pts, that is natural and gives a bit more room for short VIX. However, fo June OpEx, the VIX implies a oneâ€sigma move of Â±5.98 % (â‰ˆÂ±349 pts). By June OpEx, risk-neutral odds for SPX < 5495 â‰ˆ 18.5%, and for > 6195 â‰ˆ 8.3%This is an underpricing by 5%; not much but it is there. Note, VIX tends to underestimate the market moves, and these probabilities are derived from VIX. If my model estimates the odds right (usually does), then this is a serious mispricing, and one should monitor carefully how it evolves. Be careful, the divergence arises because the Q-measure strips out the mean-reversion drift and adds a volatility risk premium/skew. What I mean is that mean-reverting processes produce physical probabilities that account for a drift back toward a long-term mean, calibrated to historical data and true-world dynamics, while risk-neutral probabilities are derived from market option prices under an equivalent martingale (Q) measure and ignoring real-world drift in favor of no-arbitrage pricing.The marketâ€™s risk-neutral probabilities understate that physical likelihood by ~40 pp, if my model is right, but no arbitrage opportunity. The subject of this underestimationis coming from several risks. Market has run out of steam after Opex hedging flows, needs to fill key gap levels, is underpinned by rising trade deficits, tight credit/funding conditions, and inflationary supply shocks from tariffs and freight (these shocks push up costs for raw materials, shipping, and finished goodsâ€”even as demand cools, you wonâ€™t see these immediately in headline CPI; they show up first in wholesale prices, margin compression, then eventually feed into consumer prices), and likely to retest or breach prior lows before enough volatility and macro data calm down to rebuild a bullish technical structure.SubscribeINTRADAY POSTCoding todayâ€™s positioning:Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

Market analysisECB day, OpEx, Post-OpEx flows, Short Vol + Intraday post (17/April) Stagflation in the EU, Taylor ruleApr 17, 2025âˆ™ Paid231ShareWeekly post is here, Geopost is here. Very important: ECB set to cut interest rates as insurance against trade tariffs Now if we know the assumed â€œneutralâ€ real interest rate, current inflation rate, the central bankâ€™s inflation target and the real GDP nad its potential, we can assume a benchmark of how a central bank (like ECB) should set its shortâ€‘term nominal interest rate based on two â€œgapsâ€: the inflation gap (current inflation rate minus central bankâ€™s inflation target) and output gap (real GDP minus its potential): it=râˆ—+Ï€t+Î±(Ï€tâˆ’Ï€âˆ—)+Î²(ytâˆ’yâˆ—)where:Â it=Â the target nominal policy rateÂ râˆ—=Â assumed â€œneutralâ€ real interest rateÏ€t=current inflation rateÏ€âˆ—=central bankâ€™s inflation target(ytâˆ’yâˆ—)=% output gap, andÎ±Â andÂ Î²=the weights policymakersÂ choose (often around 0.5)If inflation is above target, central bank should raise rates more. If output is above potential, central bank should raise rates a bit more. If either is below, cut rates. Now, the current ECB deposit facility rate is 2.50%, the main refinancing operations is 2.65%, and the marginal lending facility is 2.90%; while this formula, called the Taylor estimator, says it should be higher by ~52.83% (around ~4.1)Now, as showed above, markets have sharply increased expectations for ECB easing, largely because 1) Trumpâ€™s erratic tariff hikes threaten euroâ€‘area growth and inflation, and 2) a rising euro exerts additional disinflationary pressure by making imports cheaper. As a result, Euribor futures and OIS curves now fully price in a 25 bp cut at the next ECB meeting, with roughly 150 bp of total easing by endâ€‘2025.Nowâ€¦ Trumpâ€™s tariff escalations combined with a strong euro are creating a classic stagflation mix (rising prices and slowing growth), yet markets are betting on ECB rate cuts. They simply donâ€™t dare to hike as much as they should. Similar trap that the US faced back in 2021/22Lose-lose for Madame Lagardeâ€¦ ECB is checkmated by Trumpâ€¦(Btw, this is totally Europeâ€™s fault. They should have raise rates since 2015, but all they have cared is the GDP growth, nothing elseâ€¦)PM OPEXLets look at todayâ€™s positioning, PM expo:This is a net positive speed profile with an overall avg slope of 2.95%But locally below 5300-5314.48 the speed profile is negative with a slope of -11.99% towards 5184.89Coding main pivot at 5305.18, this is the centroid of the whole profile. I will explain what momentum is, introduce the concept of mean reversionâ€”all to help you understand a century of observations on how the market behaves.HISTORY, BACKGROUNDThe relationship between supply and demand and its impact on prices is one of the most fundamental questions in trade, business, and economics.Modern money and capital markets exist to direct capital to parts of the economy where it is needed for production. Households, as the largest savers, invest their unused income through capital markets into companies and governments that lack funds. Like buyers in commodity markets, these investors carefully select their investments and expect comprehensive information on each opportunity. # Fed, Distribution, Revision of weekly post + Intraday post (25/June)

> Free short education on distribution

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/fed-distribution-revision-of-weekly

---

Market analysisFed, Distribution, Revision of weekly post + Intraday post (25/June)Free short education on distributionJun 25, 2025âˆ™ Paid1312ShareFedPowell reaffirmed a â€œwait-and-seeâ€ approach as expected, unwilling to look through tariff-driven price increases until clearer data on pass-through and magnitude arrive. He signaled rate-cut flexibility later this yearâ€”â€œsooner rather than laterâ€ if inflation remains containedâ€”but declined to target a specific meeting, leaning toward a September cut over July. Market prices only 15.9% probability to hold rates. A significant majority of FOMC participants penciled in cuts later this year under scenarios of subdued inflation or labor-market weakness. Also, the LEI dipped 0.1% in May 2025 to 99.0, marking its sixth consecutive monthly drop and triggering the Conference Boardâ€™s formal recession signal. Although the Board does not forecast a recession, it expects a significant growth slowdown, projecting ~1.6% real GDP growth for 2025. Fed policy moves hinge on June and July inflation prints. They arenâ€™t yet ready to â€˜look throughâ€™ tariff increases without clearer data on their size and pass-through to consumer prices.The divergence is shady. Downside remains managed for now, but Augustus window will be a surprise and pivot. SubscribeDistributionI try to explain this through tools you can also use without purchasing thousands of datas to model vol surfacesâ€¦ So, a little bit of theory here first. At the beginning there was the constant volatility and normal distribution mathematically measured by vega. But vega has got a convexity as tail OTM contracts (letâ€™s say ~5-10 deltas now) get higher risk premium, because higher change in IV elevates the risk for the seller that the contract will end up ITM, elevating the kurtosis (â€œtailednessâ€ or the propensity for extreme values) of the distribution, so as a result this draws the known vol smile. This vega convexity is called vomma or volga (vol gamma). But the distribution is not symmetrical at all, itâ€™s median, mode and mean are constantly moving. Equity markets typically have occasional large negative returns, occuring more often than a Gaussian model predicts, I wrote an educational post about it. Thus, left tail is fatter, and skewness is negative. As a result, investors and traders spend more on buying downside protection than betting on upside, and this skewness appears in the vol surface too, creating the smirk (â€œleft-skewed smileâ€ basically). What we are seeing is basically that, as we slide left or right on the x-axis (spot), we get higher/lower vol risk premium. This change of vol vs spot, and thus the skewness is described by vanna. This convexity is highest typically at the 15-35 delta ranges (I like 22 deltas to measure vanna). Here you can see the past one month, simplified: We can see leptokurtic behavior (kurtosis of 0.8605, but moderate-excess as 1 > kurtosis > 0), and also fatter right tail with skewness of 0.644542, while downside moves are clipped by mean reversion. The kurtosis was higher after Friday close and skewness was lower than today. This is the realization of the short vanna and vol of vol supply dynamics I was writing about. Scrutinizing the trend in the numbers as I code the â€œrolling realized distributionâ€, Monday was a pivotal day, squeezed right to the marked weekly level that I provided as the border of the downside gamma â€œgravitational fieldâ€, and interestingly it showed that Tuesday was a squeeze, but moderated as right tail was actually sold a bit, that flattened the short vanna regime. Tuesday post about Fed pt. 2 + Big sum and geo post Most major ports have seen 20â€“30 percent declines in 20-foot container loadings from China (TEUs booked) between Febâ€“Mar versus Marâ€“Mayâ€”steep, but nowhere near a 50â€“60 percent collapse. Some ports even bucked the trend (e.g. Miami +18 percent, Jacksonville +33 percent), suggesting pockets of resilience or rerouting. Powell & colleagues have repeatedly promised â€œampleâ€ liquidity and a willingness to step in if markets seize upâ€”softening any sell-off. The Fedâ€™s repo operations, bank liquidity lines, and extensions of FIMA swap facilities have quietly flooded funding markets with cash, keeping credit spreads tight and preventing a shipping-finance crunch. Even as real volumes dip, shipping companies and traders still get credit, which cushions the volume drop and keeps P-Lâ€™s from blowing up. This is not just the Fed in isolation. Other central banks (ECB, BoE, SNB) have been conducting back-to-back repo operations and verbal easing guidance in lockstepâ€”so trade finance stays liquid worldwide. Governments are back-stopping cargo liens, export-credit agencies are guaranteeing bills of lading, all to avoid a cascade of defaults that would amplify the volume shock.Cutting rates now would risk reigniting cost-push inflation (tariff shock still working through the system). Instead, the Fed can keep injecting liquidity to â€œpegâ€ financial conditions in a wide range, letting real-economy volumes gently drift down rather than crashâ€”and buying time until the worst tariff effects hit in mid-summer/Q3.Once those Mayâ€“June shipments (front-run pre-tariff orders) clear, youâ€™ll see real supply-chain shortages, steeper volume declines at ports, and a sharper hit to GDP and corporate earnings. Thatâ€™s when the Fed will face real â€œfight or cutâ€ pressureâ€”by then, only a rate cut (or more aggressive measures) can avert a financial-and-trade spiral.Now, think about the last days eventsâ€¦Trump told reporters on May 6 that a â€œvery bigâ€ and â€œearth-shatteringâ€ announcement was coming before his Middle East trip. Markets jumped on the hope of a positive catalystâ€”pushing ES higherâ€”but quickly retraced as no concrete details emerged. On May 7, India conducted airstrikes on militant infrastructure in Pakistan-administered Kashmir. Pakistan claimed to have shot down five Indian jets amid civilian casualties. Despite this dangerous flare-up, Indiaâ€™s rupee and equities barely budgedâ€”only to serve as an excuse to sell ES into the close, driving futures to session lows. Also on May 6â€“7, Bessent confirmed urgent U.S.â€“China trade negotiations in Switzerland over the weekend. That news immediately reversed the late-session sell-offâ€”pushing ES back upâ€”despite scant detail on the talksâ€™ scope or timeline.This is how they are messing with sentiment reading algos. Confusion over which â€œsurpriseâ€ matters most keeps algorithmic strategies stuck in volatility neutral mode, reducing the risk of a sharp Fed-induced gap. Because by mixing bullish (Trump tease, trade talks) and bearish (geopolitical risk) headlines, â€œtheyâ€ can pin ES within a tight range, discouraging directional bets just before the Fed. # Game of probabilities

> Moderate math warning!

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/game-of-probabilities

---

Educational postsGame of probabilitiesModerate math warning!Jan 27, 2025âˆ™ Paid447ShareIn the following post, I will define the concepts of determinism and stochasticism to clarify and refine the approach. I will also explain what 'probability' actually means from a mathematical perspective, as an introduction to the fundamentals of risk management.Determinism means that the final output of a process is causally inevitable. It implies that a process is entirely driven by causality, and the outcome depends solely on the variables involved. There is no randomness, and thus the system will always produce the same output from a given starting condition or initial state.For example, imagine a computer running a well-defined algorithm. It will always produce the same output for the same inputs, calculated deterministically. Our classical physical laws have also been viewed as deterministic because they are based on differential equations and function algorithmically.However, in the real world, no system is truly deterministicâ€”not even a computer. Using deterministic models is a simplification we often employ when analyzing processes at a macro level. This simplification works because it is easier and "good enough" to describe certain systems this way (e.g., the path of a thrown rock).Why is it "good enough"? Simply because complex systems tend to exhibit properties that their individual parts do not possess. When these parts interact with one another, they create a larger unit or system that produces well-observed, repeating patterns, unified behaviors, or properties that can be described deterministically. This phenomenon is known as emergence.Take the example of a thrown rock: beyond the macro level, countless variables and forces (wind, temperature, air pressure, momentum of small particles, molecules, etc.) influence its path. However, on a macro scale, the rock follows a clear trajectory that can be described reasonably well using deterministic models. If you throw it again with the same force, the path will be almost identical.Similarly, society is composed of thousands of individuals, each with their unique inner world. Yet, as a collective, society exhibits regular behavioral patterns.(This additive effect of random processes is a primary subject of chaos theory.)On the other hand, when we discuss random processes, we refer to stochasticism. Stochastic systems are entirely random, meaning there is a 50-50% probability for various outcomes, as discussed in my previous article on volatility.Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

SubscribeMeasure your portfolioâ€™s gold to real-rate beta: regress Î”gold vs Î”10y TIPS yield and vs Î”breakeven; update rolling. Adjust rates-up hedges to the beta. TY/US put spreads (short 10y/long-bond futures via puts) sized by DV01 to your estimated goldâ€“rates beta (mind vol risk!). # Important trading advice + Intraday post (16/June)

> SPX is trading at 6013 while Iâ€™m writing these linesâ€¦

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/important-trading-advice-intraday

---

Market analysisImportant trading advice + Intraday post (16/June)Jun 16, 2025âˆ™ Paid2051ShareSPX is trading at 6013 while Iâ€™m writing these linesâ€¦Here you can find the weekly post. On Friday, during the power hour I signaled that firms are buying and holding the index up. The setup into FOMC favors for reversion trades, a bit more edge on the long delta side. The key, I keep emphatizing for you, that you cannot beat the HF algos. There are two reasons for that: 1) Because, you cannot be that fast in terms of reading complex conditions, and fill trades; 2) Because you cannot be that emotionless, and disciplined. You will be distracted by win or loss.During the day there is only one intraday edge opportunity, two at max. Usually during the AM, and after 2 PM. In the first 20-30 min, intraday vol is high, bcs that when ppl are taking their 0DTE positions. You better watch during the first 30-40 min, and wait for the market to decide where it wants to go, and to build a structure (to have a retested support or resistance zone, to show its net drift, to unveil which effects are the most influential, etc.). You probably keep your spendings/incomes in check, so you know how much capital you should put into trading. If so, you should decide your daily budget, including your loss limit, and also your profit limit. Also you should decide, which side (long or short) you â€˜feelâ€™, which side you are familiar with to read, then based on the intraday setup, you should decide at what point and what conditions you will take your trade, and in what size. With this conscious planning you also determine, what conditions would disprove your trade and you take the loss. The next thing you need to do is to wait, wait and wait, until the market gives it to you. If it doesnâ€™t give it to you, donâ€™t fomo into the otherside, but wait for the next one or leave the desk, and leave it for tomorrow. You are not obligated to give your money to the market makers. The key is the careful and conscious planning, and to feel secure with it. You have to understand your decision, you have to be able to explain to yourself or to your wife, why you took that certain position. The worst that can happen is that you were wrong, and you took your loss within your limit. This is crucial, because insecurity leads to over-hesitation or to undisciplinated fomo-behavior.For fast jump-in trades, use market orders, for more calculated and patient trades, use limit orders. No trailing stop-loss. And when the trade goes into your favor, and momentum is proved, size up, and roll when the contract reaches 80-85 delta (roll back towards 50 delta). For intraday trading I like to use 60/70 delta ITM contracts due to the theta, and 50 delta ATMs when conviction is high. I use 22 delta OTMs for swing trades, with weekly expo at least. And if the MM positioning is short fly pattern, be fast out, because of higher theta bleed.Me personally, I trade a distribution, sizing based on the simulated probabilitity values, using my own modified Heston-Nandi for that, then size up on the winners and close the losers. Also DCAing only 3 times at max, when I bet on long vol (but mostly 2 legs). Reversion lvls, shared at the end of every posts, give you the probabilities. Positioning based leves are giving you the support/resistance zones, also levels where dynamics shifts. You should look for divergences. And the momentum script I shared with you, gives you a straight-forward clue about the current momentum. While the educational posts give you practical insights of risk management, position sizing, market phases and dynamics. Also showed how to create your own simulation using Excel, etc.These things are enough to beat the market. All you need to do is to draw the levels on the chart, take notes of the dynamics, and make a plan.Take your time. Be responsible and calculated. SubscribeContinue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# Increased SPX/VIX beta ahead of VIX expo - Intraday post (16/Sept)

Market cannot always be frontrunned, and predicted.Most of the times we can only determine the risk and hedge it, while simultaniously play the present moves just like we did last week with the bullish move. It is not a guessing-raceâ€¦ thatâ€™s a bad â€œfuruâ€ behaviorâ€¦The whole CPI and PPI release will reveal how much influence the government has on Fed.The question is if disinflation is happening or tariff generated pressures remain and create a sticky or even climbing inflation. Overall the market expects inflation on the front-end (1-3 year window) as a result of tariffs that will moderate later.The curve of inflation swaps are declined since QT in 2022, but seems like bottomed when the Fed first cut rates. In the April/May period, despite the recessionary and even temporal deflationary concerns, the market started to expect even higher inflation due to tariffs, and stagflationary pressures, meaning that the Fed wonâ€™t be able to handle both simultanously. I tell you what to look forâ€¦First, the market expects a neutral print, rather a hot or cold print. It expects sticky uncertainity, and most likely will wait until PPI and Jackson Hole, expanding my risk window. And OpEx will still cap the downside until. This is the marketâ€™s expectation. However, if the inflationary pressures are not significant, or even there is a small hint of cooling pressure, or turning towards disinflation (increased rate of cooling), if Sept cut happens, the market will take profit. Simply, because the Fed doesnâ€™t cut because of no-inflation risk, but because of slowing growth, political manipulation and growing debt burden. A hawkish, emergency cut, rather a green light. It would not be bearish, but correctiveâ€¦This is why the market is telling us that it is not happy about bad news at all: I wait until data releases, then reassume. # Intraday post (12/May)

> US- China

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/intraday-post-12may

---

Market analysisIntraday post (12/May)May 12, 2025âˆ™ Paid921ShareUS- ChinaThe 90-day pause in U.S.â€“China tariffs represents a truce to halt escalating duties and buy time for negotiations until Washington and Beijing agree next steps. This pause freezes further escalation, clearing away â€œnoiseâ€ so negotiations can proceed without daily headline shocks. Final outcomes now depend on bilateral diplomacy, not unilateral tariff hikes. U.S. equities jumped roughly 3â€“4% on the pause news, lifting the S&P 500 to fresh highs within days. Quant models and momentum-based funds use measured-move targets; once that objective was reached, buying interest naturally waned. Fast moves tend to exhaust one-way flows, increasing the odds of a corrective pullback that â€œcools offâ€ excessive valuations.After an up-move, a base forms when supply and demand find equilibrium, often accompanied by declining volume, creating a launchpad for a sustainable advance. Prices trading sideways within a defined corridor signal indecision and allow volume and volatility to normalize before a breakout. A retracement off the high can help wash out weaker hands and test key support levels without invalidating the uptrend. Once a pullback establishes a higher-low above critical support, buyers regain confidence for the next pushâ€”avoiding â€œblow-offâ€ tops.5760 is critical pivot momentum wise for SPX, and bullish until 5580, same with 5783 for ES=F, and 5590. Momentum wise!For more quantitatively computed ranges, check the weekly post.NO HIGH CONVICTION LONG YETâ€¦ not rushingI see that I need to explain it again. As many of you know, I short VIX, short vol, and long delta for a while as a â€œhedgeâ€ while the risk/reward is on the short side. What does it mean?SubscribeContinue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# Intraday post (19/feb)

> First, lets look at CME FedWatch tool:

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/intraday-post-19feb

---

Market analysisIntraday post (19/feb)Feb 19, 2025âˆ™ Paid1521ShareFirst, lets look at CME FedWatch tool: A quick walkthrough: Fed funds futures are quoted as 100 minus the expected effective rate: Implied Rate=100âˆ’Â Futures PriceFed always sets a target range for the fed funds rate. The FedWatch tool compares the implied rate to the current target range. If the implied rate is lower than the current range, the market expects rate cuts (or a lower effective rate), and if itâ€™s higher, it expects rate hikes.As cuts/hikes typically occur in increments of 25 basis points, the tool uses the difference between the implied rate and the current target rate (often the midpoint of the target range) to estimate the probability of a change. Of course, they consider the term structure of the futures, etc. So this way, looking at the current pricing of fed funds futures, the tool tells us how many basis points the market expects the fed funds rate to change by a given date.Here you can read their methodology more in depth. As you can see, market prices 97.5% probability for no cuts in March, 55.4% into June, and 18.6% into Dec, betting with 37% probability for a 25bps cut, and this is the highest bet. The sentiment turns in July. So basically the market only expects only one rate cut this year. Here I explained that the current higher inflation prints were natural and donâ€™t actually reflect the inflation trend. Of course, this reports are mostly fake, but we have to follow the narrative that they want to sell us, we canâ€™t be bearish if they want to boost the market and economy. The key takeaway here is that we already saw last week, that the market prices in sticky inflation, and doesnâ€™t really expect further easing. This is something to keep in mind, everything else is just temporal market speculation to collect premium and catch alpha through event vol spikes. Bad news are no news, but in an evironment like this, good news are very good news. Here I coded today gamma exposure for you:Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# Intraday post (20/feb)

> Firstly, lets modify Heston model for continuous-time Monte Carlo simulations as it provides a closed-form for the variance process as a mean-reverting square-root diffusion, and check what distribution we get for volatility for the next month based on the last 10 yrs historical data:

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/intraday-post-20feb

---

With that said, lower vol up, higher vol down. In other words: more artificial liquidity up, and less artificial liquidity down. From a momentum perspective this means stabilizing upward, and accelerating downward (left-tail risk). This is called a positive vanna environment. But also, charm is net negative, meaning dealers getting shorter delta in every five minutes, forcing them to buyback. Any deviation from these theoretical flows (that are manifested in volatility) reveal the real hands of the underlying customer flows.
