# July Monthly Outlook, Vol of vol supply, Momentum - Equilibrium + Weekly post (07-11/July)

> Momentum, Market Equilibrium, Bias, July positioning, Vol dynamics, Weekly expected dynamics, My trading strat

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/july-monthly-outlook-vol-of-vol-supply

---

Market analysisJuly Monthly Outlook, Vol of vol supply, Momentum - Equilibrium + Weekly post (07-11/July)Momentum, Market Equilibrium, Bias, July positioning, Vol dynamics, Weekly expected dynamics, My trading stratJul 07, 2025âˆ™ Paid3136ShareBearmarket odds have decreased drastically for now, even tho the risk is still not 0%. Note, that August - Sept window is still a pivot, but probability is lower. The reason is not the momentum but strictly the sentiment, because we can see that the market is aggressively held-up, and very much likely that it will remain so until the problems get solved even only on paper. Then the market will rebalance itself just like as it did after 2021. The reason for that is that the market is very vulnerable and both the economic growth and investor sentiment is dancing on the edge of the cliff. And like that, any major weakness would cause big problems. Look at SPX recovery after the COVID-crash: There were five pullbacks that where higher than 6% on the way up, cooling off the volatility by â€œwaiting for the liquidity left behindâ€, and to build a stable structure after it took back the losses by two 8-10% corrections, before started to rally. The Fed was there with QE, liquidity was stimulated. Now, look at this rebound: We only got one 6% pullback after the drawdown, but later on the way up, it didnâ€™t even performed a 3.84% pullback that would have been at least in ratio with COVID momentum. This is a different, and very aggressive regime, especially if you consider that the Fed is not fully behind this move. Using simple technical meassurements, a ~6360 blowoff top is likely before, the SPX sees a major 6-10% pullback (both offer nice buying opportunity), and coincidencing with two very important liquidity zones. Iâ€™ll tell you, why they are important, and how can this scenario play out, and what the market expects about it. But first, I need to write a bit about the mean reversion theory from a perspective to explain why your eyes can cheat you when you look at the chartâ€¦ SubscribeMARKET EQUILIBRIUMIf you have read my Educational Posts, I wrote more posts about market theories and anomalies. This is key for both analysts and traders to understand.  The market is always about to find equilibrium, but it always creates disbalances. First there is the growth, because the economy is growing, the performance is growing, the prices are growing, market is growing. But then there is the momentum effect, because of the speculative trades, leverage and the reinvestment of the leverage to create more leverage, and also because of the general sentiment that overvalues any directions. This builds a disbalance in the returns. Momentum effect is basically â€œcrowding upâ€ the right-tail or the left-tail constantly, creating a reflexive effect to realize it. And note, that historical skewness of SPX is also oscillating around its equilibrium net negative estimation! â€¦it keeps re-centering itself around its longer-term mean that is net negative holding an equilibrium skew bias, as you can see below: (You can observe how the shorter-term averages are also oscillating around the longer-term averages.) However, the market will eventually rebalance these accumulated disbalances. But not in a way that many people thinks! Because price action has two dimension: the price level axis (y-axis, vertical), and time axis (x-axis, horizontal). And just like the two sides of a right-angle, if the horizontal leg gets longer, while the vertical leg keeps its height, the slope of the hypotenuse will be lower, than if the horizontal leg would be shorter. The slopeness is mathematically measured by the vertical leg-to-horizontal leg ratio, that is the same as Newtonâ€™s formula for velocity, where horizontal axis represents the time and the vertical axis represents the displacement. And in terms of price actions, velocity is indirectly refers to volatility, and as a result it also reflects the level of liquidity (inversely, see the post). Well, market can functionate only if there is liquidity, so the providers are keep rebalancing it time-to-time, as necessary. As a result, volatility exhibits strong mean reverting behavior. This behavior is even stronger than what is exhibited by the price actions, that follow rather a positive-drifted special random walk with both momentum and mean reversion behaviors. However, this liquidity-rebalancing act that keeps the volatility mean reverting, is also reflected in the returns at some point when one scrutinizes its movements. Here you can see the relative logreturns of the SPX. (By relative, I mean that the code compares the individual returns to the average return. Simply because a daily return of 1% has different meanings if the monthly average return is 0.3% or 2.8%) On the cyan colored histogram, you can see the positive-negative imbalances of the SPX returns. Now letâ€™s look at the relative momentum of the SPX, I coded: Everywhere where the purple line crossed the horizontal, the momentum found equilibrium. I projected the price levels and dates where these modelled equilibriums occured: As you can see these are not always coincidenc with market tops and bottoms. Sometimes they do, but in a significant amount of times they donâ€™t. Why? - Because it is not the price level that rebalances itself (especially because the market is growing), but it is the liquidity! This is a common trap that many people falsely assume when they meassure a steep trend: it may be steep to your eyes, but liquidity already found equilibrium. Crucial.Now, go back to the relative SPX momentum chart (just right above this one). You see a relatively high positive disbalance. This can rebalance itself down in two ways: a high vol sudden crash (very unlikely), or simply slowing trend in time, that can be 1) choppy, or 2) upward drifting oscillation. It can take weeks until the purple line touches zero, and only after then it can start to accumulate negative disbalances (later in this post, I will tell you the level that would shift the momentum of the purple line). If you scroll back up to the relative logreturns chart, you will see a similar indications: the returns actually have some negative equilibrium (pretty counter-intuitive, right?) and both seemingly and mathematically (cyclically) seems like going back to the positive territory. (later Iâ€™ll tell you the estimated time of the cycle shift).So the key is, that price action is two dimensional. And so, the equilibrium can be found on both axis. If we look at the volatility, it definitely overshooted and accumulated some negative equilibrium, but not as much as many would first think: Letâ€™s look at its momentum: It still have room to grow. I estimate around 16.88 - 20.73% range currently, where it can grind back up. The exact magnitude would depend if vol of vol remains supplied, because then time would help to find liquidity earlier.However it could still produce a 5.79 - 7.11% pullback in the next 1.5 - 2 monthly window. The higher end is close to what is technically estimated in average.The key takeaway here is that liquidity imbalances (volatility over/undersupply) is what moves the market. News, Fed, ERs, datas, timed announcements are only there to amplify momentums on the short run (quarterly - yearly window). Events generate liquidity and liquidity generates momentum. Momentum produces gains. On the long-run however the market rebalances its valuation. This is what Benjamin Graham called as the â€œweighing machineâ€â€¦ SENTIMENT AND BUFFETT INDICATORThe bearmarket risk arises from the fact that the April drawdown was caused by investors, not traders, and the positive imbalance and overvaluation is relatively significant in a multi yearly window. As the market is held up aggressively and artificially (including pumping the â€œvoting machineâ€), even if the recession is bottomed (likely), the market will revaluate itself. Currently I donâ€™t see that Trump will let it happen. He and the banks, investement funds have every tool to make it growing even on paper until the underlying problems are tempered enough to be revealed. However, the underlying sentiment based on economic uncertainity is very pessimistic. Junk bond spreads and margin debt are grinding upward, while valuation models show extremely overvalued conditions. What I think is that bearmarket can be pushed farther in time (like into 2026), but based on cyclical analysis, I see it coming, and my estimation that at the end it will take back the fake pumped stagflationary gains since 2020 March-April.Like P/E ratio shows dot-com bubble levels, after that a multi-year bearmarket followed. This level was first hit in Dec of 2021. And just right before April, and currently trading at that. On an interest yields level, market is also overvalued, but it still has some room. (Higher interest rates lowe the profit of the firms, that will lower stock prices.) If we take sales datas we can meassure the stock price relative to the sales too (cannot be as easly manipulated as earnings). For now stocks are way overpriced relative to their sales too. Above the dot-com bubble era. The Buffett-indicator takes the ratio of the Total US Market Value to the GDP, that is â€œprobably the best single measure of where valuations stand at any given momentâ€: Buffett Indicator=MarketCapGDP=$62.79T$29.96T=0.21This means that the current value is 209.53%, signaling extreme overvaluation.The â€œbeautyâ€ of this metric is that if GDP growth slows down, the indicator will naturally rise, meaning that there is a smaller economic base for the same equity value.Beside these valuation metrics, market breadt, put/call ratio, market momentum and simple sentiment meassures show extreme greed, foreshadowing a pullback. But the valuation models show that the weighing machine is slowly turns on. In the short-term, If the market pulls back, as it should to build a stable structure and liquidity zone higher, BTC will also fall. I currently estimate it to 40-60k, but I will cover it later in a separated post. As the economy is planned to be tokenized in the next 5-6 years, I will handle BTC and ETH as an investment from here. For now, I donâ€™t make longer-term swing trades, even if the momentum points up in the very near-term. I need to see, how equilibrium is built, because momentum doesnâ€™t signals immediate pullback, risk is there and growing (especially if spot up/vol up blow-offtop happens), while revaluation is likely coming in the next yearly windows (or later this year - way less likely but not zero %). I hedge the downside risk based on my probability estimations, scaling out the short-vol and long delta positions and wait. No need to trade constantly. If I see some nice intraday setup, I jump in for a fast trade. # Market analysis

> Overview of recent events, principles, and weekly post

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/market-analysis

---

Market analysisMarket analysisOverview of recent events, principles, and weekly postApr 25, 2025âˆ™ Paid68128ShareThe risk in the current situation, then, is that the entire global security architecture has been shakenâ€”and no one can say for sure whether this tremor is merely temporary or the beginning of a much larger tectonic shift.The momentum is real; it truly has deep causes. The question is whether a transformation can happen without an earthquake. In any case, the world order will be radically reshapedâ€”both economically and geopolitically.Today I marked a very pivotal event in terms of sentiment: the Putinâ€“Witkoff meeting. And despite this morningâ€™s assassination of Putinâ€™s lieutenant generalâ€”killed by a car bombâ€”the negotiations were quite benign/promising.After the close, I once again explained why macro matters. Most people simply arenâ€™t used to a market where the fundamentals are being shaken. Youâ€™re accustomed to a well-supported market riding a growth trajectory driven by fundamental supply and demand among investors, where prices sometimes diverge (catching momentum), and then inevitably mean-revert. You learn to estimate those anomalies through options and volatility positioning, and speculate on them. (Among retails, this way of thinking is particularly widespreadâ€”mainly because most of them came up in the cryptocurrency market and therefore only know a purely speculative mindset on a purely speculative market.)But that isnâ€™t always how things work. INVESTMENT (big boys mindset) Iâ€™ve already written about the marketâ€™s fundamental driving principles. At root, these come down to the price levels at which investors are willing to buy or sell. They have various methods for determining those levels, which Iâ€™ve also outlined briefly. Liquidity providers balance supply and demand (especially against speculative flows), while investors finance companiesâ€™ operations and a countryâ€™s debt, thereby supporting growth. But sometimes a particular event will create uncertainty among investorsâ€¦When that happens, serious problems arise both in the market and in the broader economy. If investors pull out, no one remains to finance either the governmentâ€™s spending and economic growth. That leads to a general slowdownâ€”and in some cases a deflationary spiral. The withdrawal of investorsâ€™ capital causes a massive liquidity drain in the markets, driving prices sharply lower and triggering a chain reaction of margin calls. That deleveraging event then removes even more money from the economy, producing a negative-wealth effect that further depresses prices (a disinf-/deflationary force).In such situations, it falls to the government to rescue the economy through both fiscal and monetary measures. First, by deploying various emergency mechanisms (which Iâ€™ve also described briefly). And second, by restoring investor confidence. It must pinpoint why investors are withdrawing and address that root problemâ€”offering them a deal that draws them back, convinces them their capital is safe, and makes financing government spending worthwhile. It is important to note that, investors never â€œshortâ€ their finances; they always go long. To invest is to finance a companyâ€™s or a governmentâ€™s operations because you believe in its growth. In return, the company or state rewards you with â€˜dividendsâ€™ paid from that growth. (They only enter into speculative trades if theyâ€™re setting aside money for hedging purposes or if they want to make a little side income. But I can tell you for fact that most of them suck at speculative trading.)Many people simply arenâ€™t familiar with this.LAST YEARâ€™S BIG SHORT SQUEEZELast year, the market was simply being artificially stimulated. I wrote several posts about this around Februaryâ€“March. It was an election year, and the Fed openly stated that it would prioritize economic growth over fighting inflation. The surface narrative was â€œelection year,â€ but the underlying story was the USâ€“China economic cold war unfolding behind the scenesâ€”one of the very principles of the â€œTrump tradeâ€ (as I pointed out in February, Wall Street would back him to secure that victory).That rally was built on a short squeeze. Why? Because everyone by then could clearly see the economy headed toward stagflationâ€”since the Fed in 2022 had shied away from aggressively tackling supply-sideâ€“driven inflation (precisely due to the USâ€“China cold war, waiting for China to slip into recession)â€”so they were shorting the risk of an actual QT. Yet the Fed itself was effectively short OTM puts at the bottoms and short ATM calls at the tops, creating a rangebound environment. On top of balance-sheet reduction, it was also buying massive amounts of MBS. It wasnâ€™t genuine QT.Once the Fed committed to artificially stimulating growth, those shorts began to be squeezed out. Thanks to the nature of the derivatives markets, that unleashed very strong buyback flowsâ€”and that became the foundation of the â€œgrowthâ€. Speculative, highly leveraged trades piled on with the AI bubble. The market would occasionally overshoot the curve of this artificial growth, and then mean-revert. The standout event came in August, when the market unwound a large, purely speculative gainâ€”unsupported by real investor convictionâ€”after a powerful gamma squeeze. Even that, however, was ultimately just mean reversion on a higher timeframe. If you recall, the strong form of the Efficient Market Hypothesis states that prices always reflect all available information, implying no predictable patterns (such as mean reversion) should persist. Iâ€™ll briefly illustrateâ€”through my own experienceâ€”the essence of technical analysis and position analysis, explain how working people can easily determine the amount they can allocate to trading, and share my core practical principles for directional trading. SubscribeTECHNICAL ANALYSIS OR POSITIONING ANALYSIS? My personal journey on analysisThis is a very old and unnecessary debate between the two camps about which approach is more valuable. I call it â€œunnecessaryâ€ because it doesnâ€™t matter which one you use, as long as you understand the outputs. In short: technical analysis â€œguessesâ€ the important liquidity zones and probabilities based on historical data, while quantitatively monitoring positioning data, explaining the meaning behind levels and market environments, and thus gives us much greater confidence in determining probability distributions.So, the micro-cycles are determined by the volatility market, within which technical analysis applies. The volatility market helps establish the probability distribution with a high conviction level, but which trades I close and which I size up is ultimately dictated by the realized chart patterns. Fundamentals, by contrast, merely serve to supply a made-up narrative for the prevailing market dynamics. The goal is to determine the near-term risk.My long-term portfolio makes up roughly 75% of my total managed wealth. Most of it is in domestic bonds and precious metals, and about one-third in equities and cryptoassets. I use around further 15% for swing and day trading (at max!), and keep roughly 10% in cash for emergency. These are rough estimates, but I try to maintain this weighting when I take profits. For me, trading is a supplemental source of income that I donâ€™t use every dayâ€”only when I see good (high probability) opportunities. Most of my management is the continual rebalancing of the long-term portfolio. By that I mean I assess risks and set target levels at which I increase the weight of certain hedges relative to the underlying long exposureâ€”and then do the reverse once risk has proved to be declined. The goal is wealth-building, not gambling. Trading is merely a way to create new capital that I can put into further investments. This overemphasis on day trading is a harmful mindset, driven by advertising and social-media hype. A lot of people are obsessed with having the highest possible hit rate. Most intraday moves are not reliably predictable. Sometimes they are, but more often not. What matters are levels that actually mean something. A trader should plan position sizing, define a max loss per trade, and decide in advance where to close or where to scale up. The hit rate isnâ€™t what matters, risk management is. I wrote a whole educational post about it. If your hit rate is only 35%, that means 65 losing trades out of 100. Suppose you lose exactly 10 pts on each losing trade (your max loss). Thatâ€™s 650 points down. On the remaining 35 winners, you need to make only 19 points each to end up +15 points after the 100th trade. In practice you wonâ€™t always lose exactly 10 (thatâ€™s your max loss-limit here), and you wonâ€™t always win only 19 pts because you scale up your winners. The point stands: strategy matters more than hit rate.The current trend is very fast and aggressive, yet illiquid. Illiquid trends are prone to tail events (in this case, a â€œvacuum-upâ€ effect), but they donâ€™t build a durable, long-term structure. Everybody feels and meassures the lack of stability in this rally. Building long-term positions into this trend is risky.My personal decision for now is to give up some slope in my long-term portfolio growth (because increased hedging may expire worthless) until I see a 5â€“6% correction. Either we get the correction here, orâ€”if the communication is benignâ€”the next risk window is the September FOMC, because after a rate cut the market will have to reprice itself. If there is a cut, it would be a hawkish cut. By then the SPX could reach 6600â€”or even 7000. Iâ€™ll detail this in the next weekend post. And some companies have even filed preemptive lawsuits to preserve refund rights, and a secondary market has emerged where some importers are selling refund claims to hedge funds at steep discounts. Even if tariffs are invalidated, the cleanup could take months or years due to complications like documentation issues, liquidation deadlines, and possible litigation.  Bessent said that the bigger risk of an adverse ruling is losing speed and negotiating flexibility (customs revenues are now roughly $30B/month), not losing tariff revenue outright, because other legal authorities still exist  So, this will be likely a long, back-and-forth chess match, not a one-day headline.  Heavy rotation, a range-bound tape, and rising uncertainty into spring is ahead...Market expects more of this event than what its actual impact will be now. # NFP, Stagflation, Deflation, Intraday post (04/April)

> A very important shift in sentiment is on the way!

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/nfp-stagflation-deflation-intraday

---

Market analysisNFP, Stagflation, Deflation, Intraday post (04/April)A very important shift in sentiment is on the way!Apr 04, 2025âˆ™ Paid2435ShareNon-Farm Payrolls measures the net change in employment (excluding farm workers, private household employees, and non-profit organization employees). Change: Robust NFP numbers signal a strong economy, whereas significantly lower numbers indicate a cooling job market, as the overall economic demand is weak, due to declining consumer spending and uncertainty about future growth, and businesses are either hesitant to expand or are even cutting back on hiring. As job creation slows or reverses, fewer workers find new employment opportunities. Those laid off or entering the market add to the unemployment rate. With fewer people employed, aggregate consumer income and spending fall, causing the economic slowdown. When inflation is driven by supply shocks rather than demand pressures (like in 2020), unemployment can rise even as prices continue to climb. Once inflation expectations become unanchored, the economy can fall into a self-reinforcing cycle: weak NFP leads to rising unemployment, which suppresses demand further, while cost pressures maintain or even drive up inflation. Simply because prices react to supply/demand ratio. But it does matter which one is rising relative to the other.  Central banks face a conundrum: tightening monetary policy to combat inflation can further suppress growth and worsen unemployment. Conversely, loosening policy to boost employment risks fueling inflation even more. This is stagflation.My call is to get a stagflationary print. That would show very low/stagnant growth or even a contraction in some sectors. Along with weak job gains, unemployment might start to creep upward or fail to decline, indicating slack in the labor market. Although wages might be rising in nominal terms, real wage growth would be subdued once inflation is taken into account. Persistent high inflation in the face of sluggish job growth confirms that demand isnâ€™t driving robust employment gains.Now let me show you something very importantâ€¦ Something is lurkingâ€¦ Yields show that market participants are hedging downside risks and slowing economic growth. (Donâ€™t forget that the 10-year yield is a benchmark for many interest rates, including mortgages and corporate bonds. A drop means itâ€™s now cheaper for borrowers to raise funds.)Equities are down as we know by 4.84% while VIX is up by only 3.06%, with steepening backwardation: This is simply due to the complacency Iâ€™m talking about, while insiders are trying their best to avoid vol squeeze. But look at this: Gold futures are also down, lower than 3%, but itâ€™s there. It can be nothing for now, but if the trend continous, it can signal that gold becomes less attractive, since it does not generate yield. If investors shift toward holding cash or highly liquid bonds over gold, that foreshadows expactations of increase the real value of cash. Listenâ€¦When aggregate demand (consumer spending, business investment, government expenditure, and net exports) falls, prices tend to drop. Reduced spending leads to lower production, which in turn can trigger layoffs and further declines in demand. A reduction in the money supplyâ€”whether due to tight monetary policy or banking crisesâ€”reduces the amount of money chasing goods and services. (This was a central feature of the Great Depression.) This also increases the real value of debt (the debt burden does not decline with falling prices), both households and companies find it harder to service their obligations. And as real debt burdens increase, this worsens the balance sheets of borrowers and can lead to a cycle of reduced spending and further price declines.. Nominal wages donâ€™t adjust downward easily due to contracts and worker resistance. This creates a disconnect where real wages rise, further exacerbating unemployment as firms try to cut costs.This process is called: deflation. This can result in a feedback loop, when 1) reduced spending leads to lower sales and profits, prompting companies to cut costs and lay off workers; 2) with more people out of work, incomes fall, which further depresses consumer spending; 3) lower demand drives prices down even further. As prices drop, the real burden of debt increases, causing financial stress; 4) This increased debt burden and reduced income prompt even less spending and investment, which causes prices to fall furtherâ€”thus deepening the deflation. We are not there at all. We are in stagflation, where the economy growing slowly or stagnating, and prices remain high because of supply shocks.In a stagnant economy, borrowers face high real debt burdens. As Irving Fisherâ€™s debt-deflation theory explains, falling output can worsen the real value of debt, squeezing spending further. If consumer and business confidence deteriorates further, due to persistent weak growth and the burden of high debt, spending can drop dramatically. This decline in demand can eventually overpower the supply-side pressures that were keeping prices high. Once people and firms begin to expect that prices will continue to fall, they delay purchases. This behavior can trigger a deflationary environment even a spiral. See Japanâ€™s â€œlost decadesâ€.I donâ€™ believe that this gonna happen and it doesnâ€™t even need, listenâ€¦ if this trend continues, it means investor sentiment is beginning to price in the risk of deflation. That would be a very important shift!But if you understood my post about geopolitics and the global economy, youâ€™ll get that this is precisely the goal! Trump isnâ€™t trying to lure retail traders onto the wrong side; heâ€™s targeting institutional investors. He is fighting the sentiment. He isnâ€™t trying to curb monthly near-term inflation, but rather flush stagflation out of the market completelyâ€”much as Volcker did back in the day. The difference is that now thereâ€™s the unregulated derivatives market, and you have major banks, investment funds, and in the future, Arab and Jewish investors to save the market and the economy and â€œbuy the dipâ€.And from now on, all indicators and trends should be interpreted in the light of this. Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# Odds, Fair Bet and Kelly Criterion (position sizing)

> Moderate but necessary-math warning!

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/odds-fair-bet-and-kelly-criterion

---

Educational postsOdds, Fair Bet and Kelly Criterion (position sizing)Moderate but necessary-math warning!Feb 01, 2025âˆ™ Paid3351ShareIn my previous posts, you encountered the concepts of "fair play" or "fair bet." In this post, Iâ€™m going to introduce this concept in more detail, as it is one of the fundamental principles of option pricing and a key concept in risk management. I will also introduce a bet sizing mathematical model known as the Kelly criterion.  When the expected value over 100 bets is exactly zero, we refer to it as a fair bet. This means that the growth curve has no trend, or in other words, over 100 bets, the PnL theoretically becomes zero for both parties. â€œNeither the seller nor the buyer has an edgeâ€ Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

---

Market analysisOpEx Flows Pt.2 - Intraday post (19/Sept)Sep 19, 2025âˆ™ Paid7ShareYesterday evening I dropped a FREE post about the current window of risk call, investigating the story that the market is telling us, explaining the underlying dynamics: Window of Risk, Disbalance - Investigating the story that the market tellsAlmaÂ·September 18, 2025Read full storySupportive OpEx/suppressive OpEx?Supportive vanna&charm flows and so onâ€¦Is it really this simple?If something is supportive is suppressive is very much context dependent, and assuming flows by using only the OTM â€œskewâ€ contracts for schematic examples is not necessarily accurate.The supportive/suppressive is coming from the passive flows. But passive flows can be overwritten/interfered by shadow greek generated flows, and also the net exposure on a short or long speed profile is not a straight indicator to judge the vanna and charm flows because their weights can be different over the chain and tenors, generating different impacts. SubscribeI tell you an example:A negative speed profile would look like this using the schematic modelling of skew contracts: MM is net long OTM puts and net short OTM calls.Result? Net positive charm (because OTM put has positive charm, and OTM call has negative charm, but he shorts the OTM call, so his exposure is positive charm on this call as well) and net negative vanna (same reason).â€¦even tho vanna cannot be handled this wayâ€¦Is it really suppressive?If vol is sold, negative vanna would make the dealer longer delta, making him sell futures, and vice versa, while as the time passes the long charm makes him longer delta too adding another layer to sell futs.This simple positioning could be called right-tailish in itself, that is literally sells the left-tail of the curve (depending on the convexity, but here I take only this schematic exposure). So, while it tends to generate liquidity to the downside it also prevets downside vol squeezes while opens the gate for upward gamma squeezes.But where is the centroid of this setup that activate one or the other scenario? How vanna, gamma, etc. interfere with time and volatility?What if the dealer doesnâ€™t simply have these two contracts but thousands, if not millions, with different expos and different sizes?How will the exposure change then? What shape the distribution will have?Nowâ€¦ these are the questions that these oversimplified OpEx explainations doesnâ€™t give you.Iâ€™m able to code these. In this post, I share the outputs of the models and shortly explain the coded effective interactions. # OpEx Flows, supportive or suppressive vanna/charm? - Intraday post (18/Sept) 

> Supportive OpEx/suppressive OpEx?

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/opex-flows-supportive-or-suppressive

---

Market analysisOpEx Flows, supportive or suppressive vanna/charm? - Intraday post (18/Sept) Sep 18, 2025âˆ™ Paid6ShareSupportive OpEx/suppressive OpEx? Supportive vanna&charm flows and so onâ€¦ Is it really this simple?If something is supportive is suppressive is very much context dependent, and assuming flows by using only the OTM â€œskewâ€ contracts for schematic examples is not necessarily accurate. The supportive/suppressive is coming from the passive flows. But passive flows can be overwritten/interfered by shadow greek generated flows, and also the net exposure on a short or long speed profile is not a straight indicator to judge the vanna and charm flows because their weights can be different over the chain and tenors, generating different impacts. SubscribeI tell you an example: A negative speed profile would look like this using the schematic modelling of skew contracts: MM is net long OTM puts and net short OTM calls. Result? Net positive charm (because OTM put has positive charm, and OTM call has negative charm, but he shorts the OTM call, so his exposure is positive charm on this call as well) and net negative vanna (same reason).â€¦even tho vanna cannot be handled this wayâ€¦ Is it really suppressive?If vol is sold, negative vanna would make the dealer longer delta, making him sell futures, and vice versa, while as the time passes the long charm makes him longer delta too adding another layer to sell futs. This simple positioning could be called right-tailish in itself, that is literally sells the left-tail of the curve (depending on the convexity, but here I take only this schematic exposure). So, while it tends to generate liquidity to the downside it also prevets downside vol squeezes while opens the gate for upward gamma squeezes. But where is the centroid of this setup that activate one or the other scenario? How vanna, gamma, etc. interfere with time and volatility? What if the dealer doesnâ€™t simply have these two contracts but thousands, if not millions, with different expos and different sizes? How will the exposure change then? What shape the distribution will have?Nowâ€¦ these are the questions that these oversimplified OpEx explainations doesnâ€™t give you. Iâ€™m able to code these. In this post, I share the outputs of the models and shortly explain the coded effective interactions. Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

(funny enough that I market November as my next window of risk target, during the summerâ€¦)I spent April to explain you the geo situation and the motives of the sides. There I explained that this war wonâ€™t be over soon. It is a serious battle, Trump and Xi are prepared. However, note that I donâ€™t expect serious escalation until next spring.Chinaâ€™s Ministry of Transport announced a special port service fee on US-built/owned/flagged/operated vessels calling at Chinese ports, effective Tue, Oct 14. MOFCOM and GAC issued new controls on some medium/heavy rare-earth-related items and technologies, with implementation dates in November/December depending on the annex. Also the State Administration for Market Regulation opened an antitrust probe tied to Qualcommâ€™s Autotalks deal. The interesting thing is the same pattern I saw in Aprilâ€¦ that yields spiked during the after-hour or asian trading session after yields crushing moves. This makes me think that China is quietly, deliberately selling US treasuries. Including the offshore custodians, China still holds around US approximately $1.8â€‘1.9â€¯trillion in US bonds.While officially it is down by 6.44% and hit the lowest level since 2008. But I emphatize, that Beijing has hidden reserves held through offshore custodians (Euroclear in Belgium, Clearstream in Luxembourg) and stateâ€‘owned banks.So, they can treat with selling UST without significantly hurting themselves.China is preparing to inflict asymmetric pain on US highâ€‘tech industries via rare-earth restrictions, and can target active pharmaceutical ingredients, battery components and cleanâ€‘energy inputs (e.g., graphite) that are difficult to substitute, in order to choke global supply chains. Also the State Council planned to review a roadmap for yuanâ€‘backed stablecoins, with Hong Kong and Shanghai tasked to fastâ€‘track the rollout, aiming to boost global adoption of the yuan. Such digital tokens could allow Chinese firms to settle international trade without touching the dollar. Also, they launched AxCNH, the worldâ€™s first regulated offshore yuanâ€‘stablecoin in Kazakhstan, using technology from the Conflux blockchain network. These are the potential headline risks and responses from China to counter US pressure. SubscribeLetâ€™s look at the OpEx positioningâ€¦WEEKLY POSITIONINGHere you can see the weekly positioning: This is a net positive speed profile, also we have net long vanna and net short charm into the Friday expo. I donâ€™t see convexity shifts, so no fly pattern on the horizont. This speed profile means, that to the upside the gamma is increasing, and it is decreasing to the downside. The steepness is not too significant. Making it easier for the spot to climb upward, but volatility should be sold, telling us that the spot â€˜accepts the environmentâ€™. This comes hand in hand with longer vanna profile, meaning that mechanically the dealer profile becomes longer vega if price climbs upward, making them to sell volatility. And if volatility is sold, they become shorter delta, making them to buy futures supporting the spot to the upside. Meanwhile, negative charm flow makes dealers shorter delta as time passes, generating a relatively weak passive buyback flow into the OpEx. This flow will be significant on Thursday and Friday. SubscribeThe net gamma and net vega exposures of the profile are both negative.So this creates a very vol-sensitive (â€œleft-tail sensitiveâ€) positioning. This is mitigated a bit by the net long color (gamma decay) exposure, but I estimate only a 16-17% gamma increase over the week, still remaining in the negative territory. The profile is also long veta and short vomma. And this is a very important part!The long veta (vega decay) profile creates a passive and decreasing vol selling flow into Wednesday. VIX pin at 21s is very likely into VIX expo, but I will reassume it on Tuesday. However the short vomma (gamma of vega) exposure makes the profile even more sensitive to vol squeezes. (Below, I share the areas that the profile implies for vol crush and vol squeeze) However, the most important part of the profile for me is, that we are in long zomma territory! And it is even longer to the downside. Zomma is the gamma change to volatility. It is only positive at the very tails. It means vol up/gamma up, vol down/gamma down. This is a mathematically inbuilt mechanism, because the system is designed to not let volatility to be squeezed to infinity, or to be crushed to zero. Because if vol spikes, downside gammas will go up as well, flattening the speed profile and generating a â€œbrakeâ€ effect to the downside through long gamma hedging (trading futures against the direction compressing the intraday realized returns). When I see long zomma, it always tells me that market expects that the spot reached the very tails of the local curve on the scrutinized tenor. In this case, this is the left-tail: (just imagine the functions pushed lower on the y-axis until the gamma and vomma curves reaches negative territory)This is why I donâ€™t see the fly pattern, because the center would be even more higher, where vanna and speed shift to negative.So this tells me, that the market doesnâ€™t expect more downside, unless more macro trigger (China) occurs. If it does, sub-6400 is the target. Market fell 70 pts+ since. However, momentum and sentiment-wise, as long as SPX can close above 5955, ES=F above 5968.10, NDX above 21,549.18 and NQ=F above 21,603.46, pullback is not confirmed. The SPX/yields beta flipped to positive just right ahead of CPI release, as I expected on Sunday. Market reacted to softer prints as it was signaled by the beta: reacting with pullback for a softer-than-expected print. This marks a shift to a regime where yield moves are dominated by term-premium normalizationâ€”driven by fiscal deficits and heavy issuanceâ€”and anchored inflation expectations. Stable core inflation prints and clear forward guidance have convinced markets the Fed will look through term-premiumâ€“driven yield spikes unless core CPI deviates markedly, decoupling long yields from immediate policy-rate risk. Postâ€CPI data confirm real yields climbing, break-even inflation subdued, and term premium elevatedâ€”hallmarks of a growth-driven yield regime. The equity-risk premium has fallen toward zero, and valuation indicators (e.g., Shiller CAPE in the 94th percentile) warn stocks are richly priced, so positive yield shocks now often trigger a â€œcatch-upâ€ bid in equities and vice versa. Core CPI at 2.8% Y/Y and moderate headline prints have reassured markets that the Fed will â€œlook throughâ€ term-premium-driven yield spikes, decoupling long yields from near-term rate-hike risks.Nonfarm payrolls rose by 139,000 in Mayâ€”above expectationsâ€”with wage growth accelerating, underscoring broad-based labor resilience rather than a cooling trend. Despite rate-cut hopes, traders now see only a 23% chance of a July cut and 76% by Septemberâ€”down from peaksâ€”reflecting respect for labor-market resilience over headline CPI alone.The thing is that the elevation (higher lows) is an underlying trend in the beta. Markets now fear a growth slowdown or even deflationary shock more than runaway inflation, so soft economic prints (lower yields) now drag equities down. Contrary to the old â€œFed putâ€ mentality, weak data can no longer be reflexively positive because it undermines the growth cue in yields. The Fedâ€™s pivot point has moved: it cares less about term-premium ticks and more about core inflation and labor dynamicsâ€”reinforcing that only a genuine downturn will prompt cuts.And it was the real underlying concern during May, and it flipped on Tuesday-Wednesday. Letâ€™s see, if this trend continues. SubscribeContinue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

They concentrate on growth now that is equal to inflation as well now (stagflation). This is why, over the past few weeks, whenever the quant models suggested a healthy pullback, the government literally intervened and released benign announcements to trigger supportive buying. Why? Simply because the deflationary risk is real. They need to keep it going until the planned events unfold and investment interest strengthens. If you followed my April post, you know.A stronger PPI print suggests robust demand and firmer pricing power, alleviating some recession fears even as it keeps inflation risks alive. This is why we see increased SPX/LTY correlation. Soft print now means growth concerns, while strong print means growth optimism. But upside is capped due to stagflation. Rollover risk, supplyâ€shock inflation, and layoff fears are looming there that will roil fixed-income more than equities.OPEXWe have AM OpEx tomorrow, and the positioning will effect todayâ€™s price action. Charm decay favors for buyback flow, but only until SPX holds the support. Position-wise, more things points down, than up. To the upside, the key inflection point would be 5939.42, with vol up scenario. And to the downside, it is 5772.91 again, with vol up. Now, yesterday SPX close indicates a 64.81% probability for reversion, simply based on momentum only, and 63.72% for ES=F. Look at realized vol metrics: The trend is going on, and vol of vol doesnâ€™t show sign of cooling. # Recession, Stagflation, Risk + Intraday post (1/May)

> In my yesterday post you can read my insights on the coming datas and Fed. Very important.

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/recession-stagflation-risk-intraday

---

Market analysisRecession, Stagflation, Risk + Intraday post (1/May)May 01, 2025âˆ™ Paid201ShareIn my yesterday post you can read my insights on the coming datas and Fed. Very important. Bessent said de-escalation â€œis up to China,â€ because Chinese exports to the U.S. dwarf U.S. exports to China. He doubled down on a strong-dollar policy and warned Europe may need to cut rates to counter the euroâ€™s strength. Euro-zone consumersâ€™ three-year inflation expectations rose to 2.5%, up from 2.4%, indicating that the public is bracing for lasting price pressures even before tariffs bite fully. McDonaldâ€™s reported a 1% year-on-year drop in global comparable sales for Q1 2025, versus analyst forecasts of a 0.95% rise. And it keeps dropping.Weaker consumer spending and ongoing tariff escalation raise the risk of stagflation. Recent steep drops in copper futures (down 8.4% in April 2025) amid Chinese PMI weakness and tradeâ€war jitters underscore growing recessionary risks that are not yet fully reflected in broader markets. If you watch the rhetoric from the government and the Fed, itâ€™s all about recession. Why? As I wrote over the weekend, the biggest and most immediate risk is a deflationary spiral. So theyâ€™re focused on stopping that first. The market knows itâ€”speculators and mechanical flows can fuel a rally on that. Listenâ€¦Come June, itâ€™s very likely the Fed will be forced to cut rates. I emphasize â€œforcedâ€â€”not because all danger is past and growth can resume, but because theyâ€™ll have to act to avoid a deflationary spiral. Yet QE wouldnâ€™t make sense given the stagflation backdrop. The market is currently underpricing that risk. So donâ€™t expect genuine QEâ€”only as real as 2022â€™s QT was.This is a bear-market rally. And as I canâ€™t stress enough, come mid-summerâ€”when tariff-driven inflation kicks inâ€”the market is going to tank hard. Thereâ€™s no investor confidence underpinning this rally; itâ€™s purely a liquidity hunt.Youâ€™ve got to be extremely nimble and adaptable in this environment, because itâ€™s so heavily driven by policymakersâ€™ narratives. The market is mostly front-running them.But to time the decline is simply impossible now. In its Aprilâ€“June 2025 quarter, the Treasury expects to borrow $514 billion of net marketable debt, almost $150 billion more than prior estimatesâ€”assuming an end-June TGA of $850 billion. That $514 billion of new issuance flows directly into the TGA, removing an equivalent amount of reserves from banks and money markets. The Q2 marketable borrowing and the postâ€“debt-ceiling replenishment of the TGA together will withdraw hundreds of billions from reserves just as the Fed is debating rate cuts. This reserve drain tightens funding conditions, amplifying the recessionary and stagflationary pressures youâ€™ve already identifiedâ€”and puts the Fed under even more pressure to ease despite sticky inflation.Two-year Treasury yields have already dropped below the Fedâ€™s funds rate (4.25â€“4.50%), signaling markets expect rate cuts imminently. On the day jobless claims jumped, the 10-year yield slipped to 4.15%, and S&P 500 futures ralliedâ€”classic sign that weaker labor data leads to lower yields and risk-asset inflows. Powell has said rate cuts depend on clear signs of rising unemployment and slowing GDPâ€”â€œonly if unemployment hits and GDPâ€ targets are met. With the unemployment rate still near 4.2% and claims elevated but not crisis-level, immediate yield drops are manageable. However, further jobless jumps could force two-year yields down by 100+ basis points in a matter of weeksâ€”amplifying funding-market stress and potentially triggering margin calls in leveraged sectors. A large, rapid yield decline can disrupt money-market funds, bank balance sheets, and short-term funding, compounding the recessionary drag of rising unemployment.INTRADAY POST Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# Review of One Year's Performance | A year has passed

> Review of the first year of my substack

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/review-of-one-years-performance-a

---

Review of One Year's Performance | A year has passedReview of the first year of my substackFeb 03, 2026âˆ™ Paid3183ShareA year has passed. Iâ€™d like to thank all of you for your supportâ€”both moral and financialâ€”and for the trust youâ€™ve placed in me.So let this post stand here as a reflection on the period behind us: Iâ€™ll review my own performance, share a few small updates, and announce an anniversary subscription discount.IntroductionItâ€™s been a year since I launched this Substack page, after a Twitter thread I posted in January 2024 about the dynamics of a vanna squeeze suddenly led to a surge of new followers. Over time, I increasingly felt that I wanted to shareâ€”on a daily basisâ€”how I prepare for day trading ahead of the U.S. market open; back then, I often shared my trade signals as well.Even then, some people tried to discredit me in various ways in pursuit of their own business interests; others offered me workâ€”or outright plagiarized my content. My analyses were bold and accurate (with the exception of the Juneâ€“July 2024 gamma squeeze, when I was adapting on the fly). After only three months, hundreds of you asked me to create my own platform. When I didnâ€™t, by the autumn of 2024 many of you were asking me to at least set up a â€œBuy Me a Coffeeâ€ page so you could show your appreciation for my work.In the end, my Substack launched in early February 2025, and in a single year it has grown to more than 2,500 subscribersâ€”without any targeted marketingâ€”despite shadow bans, divisive topics such as my geopolitical outlooks, and continued attempts to undermine my credibility.Among my subscribers youâ€™ll find a wide range of people: from high-ranked senior fund managers and economists, to market makers, to individual retail speculatorsâ€¦ a truly wonderful, diverse community.This year has challenged me in several ways, too. I had to learn how to tailor my thoughts and analyses as optimally as possible to the needs of roughly two thousand subscribers, and to communicate them clearly and in a structured way.That took time on my partâ€”and patience on your partâ€”since Iâ€™m inexperienced when it comes to services like this. But I think weâ€™re on the right track.Thank you to those of you who believed in me.Iâ€™ve had private conversations with a number of them to ask what they find compelling about my work. Their feedback has been strikingly consistent, and it almost always boils down to three key points:1) I donâ€™t compromise my integrity. During the April crash, a lot of traders arrived from other Discord servers looking for a â€œfree lunch.â€ Even then, I stayed grounded in a probability-based framework and didnâ€™t jump onto the popular finfluencer/guru bandwagon. I accepted the statistical consequences of that choiceâ€”and the reality that some of those people would leave disappointed and then spread lies about me.2) A stochastic, probabilistic mindset. Although my models can anticipate trends with high accuracy, I avoid absolute statements and I do not introduce bias that couldâ€”even subconsciouslyâ€”impair readersâ€™ judgement. Day after day, I explain and remind everyone of the marketâ€™s reality: in markets we bet on probability distributions, and we hedge probability distributions. I explicitly acknowledge that outcomes are conditionalâ€”and that an analystâ€™s job is to identify those conditions and the highest-probability scenarios implied by them, not to â€œpredictâ€ in a fortune-telling sense. The market is only highly predictable when the confidence level is genuinely highâ€”and the underlying conditions are clearly defined.3) Breadth of analysis. I track both macro context and micro-level dynamics. I donâ€™t overemphasize either; instead I adjust the weights of different determinants as their relevance changes. This is hard workâ€”because, as one fund manager put it, Iâ€™m effectively doing the work of an entire research-desk team on my own.+1) Value for money. Some of you also emphasized that while the macro and micro data I work with are expensiveâ€”and processing them requires serious work and learning, not to mention building my individual models and evaluating the data (the product of a lifetime of study)â€”the subscription price remains significantly lower than what you would expect, especially given the accuracy. And thatâ€™s true: I initially shared everything for free, and today I charge a friendly price because this is not my full-time jobâ€”and because I want people with smaller savings to be able to be here as well. So I set what I consider a minimal, fair fee for the work.Changes and outlookAs I promised at launchâ€”and repeatedly over the past yearâ€”I am increasing the subscription price. This does not affect existing subscriptions (just as the switch to euros didnâ€™t). From now on, new subscribers can access the analyses for â‚¬52 per month. There will be no further increases.However, until the end of February, a 35% discount will be available, bringing the cost for the next year close to the previous price. You can access this offer via this link. Iâ€™ve also launched a referral program: youâ€™ll receive a unique referral link, and new free subscribers who join through it will count as â€œreferrals.â€ You should have received a separate automated email about this.Beyond that, more educational posts are comingâ€”some in video format. I have a strong accent, so be prepared. Iâ€™m also planning short free podcasts on general market topics, geopolitics, and themes many of you have asked me to cover, such as the technical and philosophical perspectives of artificial intelligence.My models are under continuous development. I plan to recalibrate some models already in use and to build new ones (i.e., TradingView indicators for you). I have a lot of ideas.Originally, I planned to run this Substack for 1â€“1.5 years. At this point, Iâ€™m confident I can commit to the full 1.5 years. After August, weâ€™ll see what happens. I donâ€™t know how the next year will unfold, but with some of you Iâ€™ve agreed thatâ€”at worstâ€”I will continue publishing the weekly posts and sharing the macro and educational content.And now, Iâ€™d like to examineâ€”statisticallyâ€”how my analyses have performed over the past yearâ€¦Annual performance reviewI wonâ€™t share the exact code, because it is heavily AIâ€‘based. And in any case it requires a special computer setup, and on top of that I didnâ€™t write the neural network, claim graph, argument graph, assumption layer, etc. parts, because I donâ€™t have the expertise for thoseâ€¦ yet.Since a large part of the code is not my work, I canâ€™t make the backtest method fully transparent; however, at the end of this letter I will attach, in a zip file, the downloaded posts, and you can test them yourselves as well. This file will of course only be available to paid subscribers.In the backtest I examined the accuracy rate. Because my analyses are mostly conditional, I looked at what percentage of the dynamics assigned to price levels and ranges actually materialized. I also included the volatility environment in this.In addition, I extracted and backtested my predictive statements about macro processes separately on the longâ€‘term and shortâ€‘term timeframes. I examined, on the one hand, to what extent my forecasts were consistent with the available news and researchâ€‘desk publications, and on the other hand I also looked at how much time elapsed on average between my forecast and the forecasts on the same topic from the research desks available to me, and between the publicly documented start of the real processes and the forecasts. Then I calculated in what percentage of cases I was a frontâ€‘runner. Here, however, it is very important to keep in mind that quite a few forecasts have not yet materialized, so I also included those publicly available highâ€‘quality reports that warn that the process is beginning.When analyzing the weekly analyses, two problems arose. First, the sample is very small, so statistically it is difficult to draw significant conclusions from them. Second, I only started describing the expected weekly dynamics in a systematic and consistent way a few months ago, but I did not have sufficiently highâ€‘quality historical data to backtest these precisely. Therefore, for the weekly analyses the margin of error is very high, but the trend is well describable.I also did not include last yearâ€™s intraday trading signals in the backtest. Any subscriber can view these in the dedicated chat and backtest them.So the conclusion is that the edge of the intraday posts does not primarily come from the raw directional hitâ€‘rate, but from the fact that around the specified levels the market behaves according to the functions assigned by me.Here are the results:Intraday posts:The average hit rate of the levels derived from the speed profile is 64.85%, which includes both the levels themselves and the realization of the dynamics assigned to them.With a 95% CI, even the lower bound of the data is above 55%, and 27.91% is greater than or equal to 75%.Performance is driven by the levels and the dynamics assigned to them.However, there is an approximately âˆ’0.099 correlation with volatility, which means that during higher volatility performance deteriorates slightly. In calm market environments there were several days when the forecast was 80â€“100% accurate, i.e., it practically indicated that dayâ€™s move perfectly. In contrast, on very hectic daysâ€”when market volatility was exceptionally highâ€”the intraday hit rate dropped.Script inputs:As the inputs are not adjusted for skewness and kurtosis yet, we can expect that the general fat left-tail will distort the results towards underestimation.Expected 68.27% - realized 46.01%Expected 95.45% - realized 76.07%Expected 99.73% - realized 89.57%Expected 99.99% - realized 96.32%So the projected sigma most often underestimated the realized dispersion in the sample. This was distorted especially by the April events and the switch to Globex, because overnight price actions often generated significant gaps.However, after a downside touch, reversion occurred on average in 55.26% of cases. In the upside case, 36.59%; this characterizes the leftâ€‘tailed distribution well.Return to the geometric mean of the reversion bands after a downside hit occurred on average in 42.87% of cases, whereas after an upside hit only in 25.04%, and after an upside hit dispersion is higher and skewness is positive, which means that the higher the upside reversion band spot touches, the less likely spot is to return to the mean. In the downsideâ€‘hit case this dispersion is much smaller, and even at the lowest level it is above 33%. So the probability of downside level reversion is significantly higher.Weekly post and ranges:Upper level hit = 84.62%Lower level hit = 23.08%Trend shift (for the next week) after breaking the level = 69.23%Reversal after hitting lower level = 66.67%So the levels to the upside signal trend reversals wellâ€”exactly what they are meant to do. The lower levels most often function as reversion levels, but the sample size is small.The posts containing the weekly ranges also usually hinted at whether an upside or downside breakout from the range is expected, and whether overall I expect a bullish or bearish week. In this respect the hit rate turned out to be surprisingly high, around 66â€“69%. Based on the data, out of 13 weekly forecasts I correctly anticipated the next weekâ€™s trend direction in ~9 cases.Risk levelsThis is the strongest part. The definition of a risk level is that it is a trendâ€‘fail filter, a kind of â€œmake it or break it level.â€ So if price breaks through it, we can expect the trend to continue; if it rejects it, the trend does not continue.In the examined sample, acceptance at the upper risk was 62.90%, and at the lower risk 45.16%.When acceptance occurred, the dayâ€™s direction practically switched in a regimeâ€‘like way. In the case of upside acceptance, the mean return was +0.643% and the upâ€‘day ratio was 94.87%, while in the case of lower acceptance the mean return was âˆ’0.881% and the upâ€‘day ratio was only 10.71% (i.e., roughly 89% was a downâ€‘day). This behavior fits the â€œmake it or break itâ€ interpretation exactly: with risk acceptance the day enters trendâ€‘mode, therefore the classic meanâ€‘reversion expectation (e.g., back to Î¼) weakens significantly. The average Closeâ€‘toâ€‘Close effect for the next day was already more mixed (after upper hold average +0.290%, after lower hold average +0.418%), and it suggested that after lowerâ€‘risk acceptance a bounce often appears, whereas after upperâ€‘riskâ€‘level acceptance a more moderate followâ€‘through is seen.Macro predictions: Here the distribution is about 80% in the 0.6â€“0.8 band and 18â€“19% in the 0.8â€“1.0 band. The average is about 70.09â€“72.8%.My accurate forecasts preceded public sources by about 33% on average.61â€“71% of the accurate predictions appear before events with a median lead time of 56â€“61 days. (In the case of shortâ€‘term statements, this number disperses toward 77.7%.)These are extremely high ratios, especially in an area where predictability is made difficult by many unexpected factors (wars, election outcomes, diplomatic turns, energyâ€‘policy developments, regulatory changes, etc.).Time evolution:All performance fluctuates, including mine. The magnitude of the fluctuation began to subside in the first half of May, and performance started to increase. This is distorted, however, by the fact that in the first period the intraday posts did not yet have a refined, standardized format. Toward the end of the year there were fewer exceptionally lowâ€‘accuracy (20â€“30%) days, and high hitâ€‘rate (above 70%) days became more frequent. For weekly posts, performance rather stagnates at the levels described earlier, but it was strongest during the summer. Similarly, the accuracy of the geopolitical analyses also stagnates at high levels. So here a consistent methodology is present.Overall:The general average accuracy rate that can be converted into a trading edge falls between 59.64% and 61.38%, depending on weighting (equal weighted vs. levelâ€‘plusâ€‘dynamics weighted information). But a friend of mine calculated a 59.20â€“65.29% interval. Methodâ€‘dependent. It is important to keep in mind that this is not a classic winâ€‘rate (not â€œ6 wins out of 10 tradesâ€), but a 0â€“100 match score (combined score) that measures how closely the â€œmapâ€ described in the post played out in the market. This score simultaneously includes whether the specified levels actually came into play and how much the behavior/dynamics assigned to them (pivot reaction, centroid â€œmagnetâ€ characteristic, targetâ€‘fulfillment characteristic) were confirmed, and (if present) the bias/direction component as well.So if we hold everything constant, then, simplified, out of 12 months my analyses will be useful in 7.26 months, i.e., out of 252 trading days on 152 days the analyses will be accurate. So about 5 months, i.e., 100 days, when the analysis doesnâ€™t help much. However, the degree of accuracy also fluctuates along a spectrum, the median of which is 58.33% (dispersing upward). On the other hand, under a more conservative model (signâ€‘onâ€‘returnsâ€‘type, with the absolute value of normal returns) it indicates about a 1.84â€“2.54 annualized Sharpe, but under a more optimistic estimate (symmetric +1/âˆ’1 payoff for each signal, assuming a daily trade) 3.17 can also be derived. Both are significantly high values.(For comparison, the general accuracy rate of research desks is fluctuating around 40â€“50%.)I know this isnâ€™t the 85-90% accuracy rate that most furus claim for themselves, but at least itâ€™s true and well-auditableâ€¦The strongest points of my analyses, in order, are the macro context/bias, risk level, the speed profile levels (including weekly dynamics). Compared to these, the weekly ranges and reversion bands are weaker, but the accuracy still mostly disperses above the average, just only slightly. Some of my more important predictions from the past yearLetâ€™s review a few of my higher-impact predictions from the past yearâ€¦Februaryâ€“April window â€” I flagged the April drop days in advance, with then-unbelievable downside targets that were hit. Three days before it I calculated the bottom level to within 100 points (which, at 35.02% realized volatility, is well within a 1â€‘sigma daily range), and I said that this crash was one of the first signs of geopolitical restructuring â€” a â€œweighing machineâ€ momentum in Grahamâ€™s sense (not simple speculation).I predicted â€œhidden QEâ€ through Bessentâ€™s bond market operations, and the rebound even if I didnâ€™t expect such a sharp/fast rebound.Geopolitics -  I dedicated tons of posts for Trumpâ€™s geopolitical program: that there would be a USâ€“Russia rapprochement via Gulf mediation, and that the EU would be pushed toward a national economic model (which is why they will run to China for help) â€” and all of that is now happening (see NSS for example). Furthermore, with sudden events, I helped interpret themâ€”something that the majority of renowned and highly followed analysts failed to doâ€”and my interpretations ultimately proved correct (like UA-peace plan, Trump-Putin meeting, Venezuela, US-EU conflict, or Warshâ€™s plans).Correction in May â€” My primary WoR ran from the May ECB rate decision to mid-May (Trumpâ€™s visit to the Middle East). We saw a ~97-point decline in SPX, but the market did not break my target level from which momentum could have flipped into left-tail risk.(Later, a 124-point decline materialized after Trumpâ€™s visit, but that wasnâ€™t the risk I expected; it followed directly from weekly positioning with an exact target. The same applies to the 107-point decline in the 16â€“20 June week.)July/August window â€” Essentially my original major WoR: the post-NFP and FOMC period, once tariff effects began to show up in the data. I marked it into mid-August. The effects did start to appear, and we got a 215-point declineâ€”still not a break of the level that would have triggered the left-tail risk Iâ€™d been flagging.(In mid-August another 138-point decline followed, but again the structure held. By mid-July it had also become clear to me that the market would remain supported, and I emphasised that my WoR was shifting towards the post-September OpEx period.)Bitcoin trend and levels - Back in July, I predicted the Bitcoin local top to happen in October, marking it as the highest probability top.Gold up - In early August I forecast that a new momentum phase would begin â€” back when gold was still only at $3500 (and I warned my subs for the selloff of the speculative rally, one day before it happened). Market analysisSentiment check - Intraday post (26/Dec)Dec 26, 2025âˆ™ Paid9ShareWeekly post: GDP Release - Weekly post (22-26/Dec) [FREE post]AlmaÂ·December 20, 2025Read full storyShort-term sentiment is still â€œgood news are not too bad newsâ€ almost neutral.The past few days the market was heavily manipulated via ICs on a thin tape.In the following days we will see if the sentiment has actually shifted and market is ok with the GDP, or they need to reprice the easing cycle expectationsâ€¦In the wednesday post I wrote:â€The dice are loadedYesterday the GDP and PCE datas came out how I expected: inflationary tendencies (re-accelerating actually) and â€˜GDP upâ€™, and deficit remains but it decreased a bit, while investment also decreased. Market indeed started to reprice Fed cut expectations.In the weekly post, I said, that the release will be an important sentiment-check on the mid-run. In the premarket post, I marked that it seems like, they want to hold the market up:1) on Monday close, the SPX/yields beta went up towards zero. It didnâ€™t go positive, but it flattened the market reaction;2) we had an IC 10x times the trade size of the average position sizes, that was set just right at pivots where long downside vol positions had convexities;These two in themselves dampened the initial market reaction that was a 15 pts sell in futures, and then forced algos to bid on delta right at the open.  In regards of the weekly post, this is a discrepancy of what the market expected for the week. This is where the edge lies, because:1) If the market behaves as it is expected, then it is an easy play, sentiment remains in check, volatility remains controlled, even if the expectations are negative;2) If the market moves more above the expectations, then the sentiment is out of control (more bearish/bullish), reinforcing the trend;3) If the market divergences from the initial expectations, thats the strongest signal, because that also means that the initial positioning must be shifted providing mechanical support too. However, the trend must be confirmed!Now, Friday will be the tell if the GDP reaction was an actual shift in sentiment (even if forced) or just the temporal product of IC and mechanical flows solely on a thin tape.SubscribeAfter all these, Trump posted: This post felt literally just like if he would have answered to my last two posts I dropped during the weekendâ€¦There are some small fibs about trade deficit, investments and inflation, but the core message aligns with my â€œBessent putâ€ thesis I just wrote about few days ago, that they are about to reshape the reaction function to the economy. And when the POTUS says something like this, you better take it serisouly!Trump claims that in good old days, markets were rallied on good news. Depends on what he meant by â€œold daysâ€: after the dotcom bubble, it was net true until 2020, however before 90s it was exactly the opposite. As long as core inflation remains above target, market doesnâ€™t like â€˜good newsâ€™â€¦ ot it should not doâ€¦However what Trump promises by this post is that they will rig the game even more fundamentaly in order to keep the markets up. UK and US both did something similar in the 70s, and also Turkey did so two years ago driving their economy into a hyperinflation. All of these ended up in forced tightenings after.Whatâ€™s the purpose? Well, if you get GDP up/prices up and policymakers still try to push easier conditions, the best case is a â€œfinancial repressionâ€ with lower real rates that erode the real burden of debt, but at the cost of higher inflation volatility and distortions.However there is a risk that long-end yields still rise, and so debt service can worsen. And this is were the Bessent-put comes into playâ€¦They expand long-end buyback operations and aim to keep vol low. But this will keep inflation sticky again. This tells me that Trump wants to bring back the post-WW2 â€œyield curve controlâ€ scheme later.The Fed implemented a form of yield-curve targeting, capping rates at multiple maturities (famously ~2.5% on long bonds). This made mortgages, corporate loans, and other investments cheaper, stimulating the economy.The market rallied like ~135% from 1942 to 1951 March, when they unwound this regime. Because once inflation pressures rise, holding down long yields can require balance-sheet expansion and/or regulation-like controls, which can undermine credibility and create an eventual break. Now, you can see a three-years long bearmarket started in 1946 May.This was because of sharp reflation after price controls were eliminated. As a result of YCC, bill yields pegged at 0.375% and the long bond capped at 2.5%, with the Fed having to buy securities to defend the structure if markets didnâ€™t want to hold them at those yields. Now, even if nominal yields were anchored, real yields could become sharply negative during the inflation spike, creating a noisy valuation environment.Later, as inflation expectations persisted, Fed went for tightening in 1948, and the economy entered its first postwar recession in Nov of 1948, and then the cycle reset. We have however this AI-boom too, that is supportive only if the developments deliver real productivity, shifting the environment towards positive supply-side shock. But the risk is high that the AI is demand today but wonâ€™t become a supply tomorrow. Debt/GDP could look better in nominal terms, but productivity would be lower and capital allocation would degrade. And this would produce a very similar or â€œrhymingâ€ pattern of what happened in the last century. On the short-runThis will hold up the markets artificially and win the mid-term elections at any cost.The game is rigged, I donâ€™t consider it as a good investing environment into risk assets, nor for longer-term trades. Iâ€™m scaling out of some longer-term positions, took profit from Nov 20 longs, and will take new ones only if a pullback happens, potentially around spring. In my â€œplaying the AI bubble postâ€ I marked 8k+ target as max for the cycle under Trump. I will refresh this post soon. As the market is rigged, I do not play any downside, as I keep emphatizing. When I see risk, I hedge volatility solely, not spot direction. I use the dips to take long positions, but I see no edge in shorting, not because it canâ€™t go down, but because the market is heavily manipulated towards teh upside until reality hits. # Statistical-arbitrage: Mean Reversion Trading

> Course: Cointegration, ADF, CADF, Johansen-test, Risk management

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/statistical-arbitrage-mean-reversion

---

Educational postsStatistical-arbitrage: Mean Reversion TradingCourse: Cointegration, ADF, CADF, Johansen-test, Risk managementOct 24, 2025âˆ™ Paid2722ShareIn the following educational post, I will examine mean-reversion trading. I will introduce cointegration, the Augmented Dickeyâ€“Fuller test, the Johansen test, the mathematics of half-life, and my own method for incorporating the realised distribution (skewness and kurtosis).I will explain the principles of risk-management strategies and show practical techniques for applying this highly creative market-maker strategy.Finally, I will touch on the core idea and mathematical approach of a cross-sectional long/short strategy. An in-depth course for tradersâ€¦SubscribeINTRODUCTIONIn my most recent educational post, I explained how, over the long run, most retail traders end up with a negative expected value. Alongside pricing, costs and taxes, one of the most important drivers of this is the random movement of prices, which materially reduces the probability of accurate forecasting. Matters are complicated further by the fact that the very probability of being able to predict price moves also fluctuates over time.Market inefficiencies can be exploited by a trader only until they become widely known. Once they are, the edge disappears. A classic example was the January effect: toward year-end, investors and fund managers would sell their smaller-cap stocks, partly to optimise the look of their year-end reports and partly to realise losses to reduce their tax bills. That selling created pressure on small caps, which the big players then bought back after the forced sales. As this anomaly became more widely known, more traders started snapping up the small caps being dumped onto the market; the January effect largely vanished, and brokerage costs could swallow the entire profit.Price dynamics have several mathematical properties that sharply distinguish them from a pure random walk. These include heavy left tails and negative skewness in returns; volatility clustering; mean reversion and long memory in volatility; long-memory in order flow (stemming from order-splitting, while prices remain near-martingales because liquidity providers offset it); momentum and mean-reversion tendencies, and so onâ€”I have written about these before.Algorithms exploit such anomalies as well, so any edge is highly unstable. Bond-market prices, in particular, are literally unpredictable.As I noted in my last post, there are three things we can say with confidence: 1) time passes; 2) volatility mean reverts; and 3) over the long term, equity indices are very likely to rise (though I have already shown counter-examples). This favours long-term investors and volatility sellers.So how could one build a strategy with a positive expected value even in the long term?In the chart below you can see the SPXâ€™s deviation relative to its monthly average: This is not volatility, but it is very similar, since volatility measures the standard deviation of log returns over a given window. Yet, like volatility, it exhibits a well-defined mean-reversion property. Because of this, a misconception has taken hold among retail traders that deviations from the 21-day moving average reliably signal an overextension in the direction of market momentumâ€”something that can be exploited with various â€˜fade-the-moveâ€™ strategies.At first glance the chart would seem to bear this out; the problem is that the moving average itself is continually moving. And that overlooks the role of time correction: the longer the market spends at a given level, the more the moving average will converge on that level. Thatâ€™s high-school mathematics. Thus the moving average can move towards spot, and spot can move towards the moving average; on the chart above, the two will look identical. That gives an edge to volatility mean-reversion trades, but for spot traders it poses the same question yet again.The problem is unchanged: the average movesâ€”and it can move either up or down. What we would want is an â€˜averageâ€™ like volatility has, something that is roughly estimable. The problem is the uncertainty of direction. Now, spot has no such thingâ€¦ but we are able to generate one ourselvesâ€¦ Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

July sentiment manipulation + Intraday post (27/June)Jun 27, 2025âˆ™ Paid182ShareWeekly postRevised-weekly post on dynamics and FedLooming Fed pivotTail wags the dog, or dog wags the tail, or these are both the same as Jung would have say?
