The key point is sentiment. That is artificially held up on dovish hopes. Both Fed, Bessent and algos made market taking bad news as good news. To fully understand this, you have to remember what Iâ€™m always telling you: when you listen to the news, always ask the question â€œwhy now, what do they want to sell me?â€, bcs on the Street sentiment is also literally a product. Always watch the timing first, not the content. Content only matters in the context. Read about the terms of â€˜reflexive controlâ€™. And the other thing is, that the whole system is just numbers going up and down. There are dynamic correlations (positive/negative) between different metrics etc. that algos are constantly monitoring (especially the trend of the changes!!) and so they trade based on it. So, this way, the tail starts to wag the dog, while the dog also wags the tail. A reflexive, self-fulfilling system. And a strong hand from the outside, like Bessent, can add other trends, values to this machine, that shifts the algo sentiment a bit. (I keep talking about the â€˜hidden QEâ€™ since April 13th, since SPX was at 5363.35). The reason for this sentiment manipulation is that they know that tariff effects ad other supply chain problems that I painted for you in April, are about to come, and will slowdown growth. Not necessary recession, 40-45% I assume only, but a strong, persistent stagflation. But Trump is about to pump growth for any price, as much as he can until reality starts to loom in. So, sentiment is prepared to take recessionary datas positively. Pricing in a dovish tilt into July FOMC. If the Fed would actually cut rates, t-bill yields would fall, making money funds less attractive, triggering an outflow from MMFs into equitites. If no-cuts happen, dollar would strenghten and we would see a slow, coordinated sell. This is a pivotal event if we are in a bear market or not. Remember the stagflation rally of the 70s... # Tariff war, Bipolar World, Recession, Weekly post (31th march - 4th April)

> Geos, Tariffs, Politics, Volatility, Underpriced left-tail

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/tariff-war-bipolar-world-recession

---

Market analysisTariff war, Bipolar World, Recession, Weekly post (31th march - 4th April)Geos, Tariffs, Politics, Volatility, Underpriced left-tailMar 30, 2025âˆ™ Paid46138ShareIn this post, I will explain the relationship between the EU and the US in the context of the tariff war, and what risks the transforming world order is facing. From this, you will also understand the direct and indirect impacts all of this has on the markets.It is important to grasp the underlying mechanisms, because the market reacts to them. Take the time to go through it. Unfortunately, many people do not understand these things Iâ€™m writing about. They donâ€™t talk about themBefore we look at how the market is pricing itself for the week, there are two important things to mention. The first is the worldwide geopolitical realignment. This is very significant, because the economic framework weâ€™ve known until now is changing fundamentally, and in this context, all sorts of market trends make sense. The second is the impact of the JPM collar.Geopolitics again...I previously described the scenario intended to lay the groundwork for the modern â€œHoly Alliance,â€ so that peace may be achieved between the U.S. and Russia. Its foundation is ideological.I also mentioned that this realignment has ideological roots. Liberalism in the West has degenerated into a kind of self-serving hedonism, which, lacking a guiding ideology, inevitably leads to a collapse of the system over time. On the other hand, left-liberal political forces have monopolized the concept of democracy, and people have forgotten that democracy can exist within a right-wing conservative system as well. In fact, in history, the only functioning democracy was based on strongly ideological and conservative foundations.The core of any political ideology is what it believes about human nature. This is the basis on which we distinguish the left from the right. On the left, the individual is at the center, and everything elseâ€”nation, religion, institutions, etc.â€”exists to serve the individual. On the right, the individual is also central, but here the individual submits to those preceding and outliving them (nation, religion, ethics, etc.).The far-right extreme is nationalism, and the far-left extreme is communism. Since theyâ€™re extremes, both create total systems, but their ideological core on the wings is the same, so when the conflict between political ideologies becomes sharper, the right and the left donâ€™t cooperate with each other; rather, they gravitate toward the far right or far left, forming alliances there.Thatâ€™s why we see the shift among the Republican U.S., Russia, and right-wing European states toward the Arabs and other nationalist states, and among the Democratic U.S. and globalist European countries toward communist China and its allies.This transformation isnâ€™t yet obvious, but itâ€™s visible, and itâ€™s crucial to understand it in order to make sense of current events and future movements.Re-bipolarization, economics re-structurizationTrumpâ€™s tariffs are intended to force the EU to the negotiating table, compelling it to accept the U.S.â€“Russiaâ€“Middle East peace proposal. The globalist elite rejects this, since it would lead, among other things, to bleeding out the global capitalist systems, meaning they need to look for new allies and sources.In the charts below, you can see that after the first Trump administration (and the tariff war), a diversification trend began, in which both the EU and the U.S. started to increase the number of supplier countries for strategic products.The EUâ€™s diversification aimed to reduce dependence on Russia, while imports from China grew. The U.S., on the other hand, aimed primarily at independence from China but also from Russia. However, this comes with increased costs. In the first months of 2025, the Trump administration enacted several significant tariffs that directly hit European exports:On March 12, 2025, tariffs of 25% on imported steel and 25% on imported aluminum took effect, applying globally with no country exemptions. The official justification was national security: the U.S. argued that reliance on foreign metals threatened its industrial base, a claim made under Section 232 of the Trade Expansion Act. European officials criticized this rationale, noting it â€œdisregard[s] the national security imperatives of the United States â€“ and indeed international securityâ€ when used as a pretext for protectionism. The EU had actually suspended its prior counter-tariffs on U.S. steel/aluminum during negotiations in 2023, but reactivated plans to retaliate once these new 2025 metals tariffs hit.On March 26, 2025, President Trump announced a sweeping 25% tariff on all imported automobiles (passenger cars and SUVs) and automotive parts. This import tax on cars, scheduled to begin April 3, 2025, is expected to affect major European automakers (Germany, France, Italy, etc.) which count the U.S. as a key market. The White House estimated the auto tariff could raise about $100 billion annually in revenue. The official reasoning given was economic: to foster U.S. domestic manufacturing and reduce reliance on foreign auto imports. By making imported cars 25% more expensive, the administration aims to push production and supply chains back to American soil (though industry experts warn it will also raise prices for consumers and disrupt supply lines for U.S.-based carmakers).In a more unconventional step, President Trump ordered a 25% tariff on any country that imports oil or gas from Venezuela in early 2025. This measure, leveraging the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA), was ostensibly aimed at pressuring NicolÃ¡s Maduroâ€™s regime by penalizing its foreign customers. In practice, it swept up some EU countries (notably Spain and the Netherlands) which historically process Venezuelan crude. Those countries suddenly found all their exports to the U.S. taxed an extra 25% as long as they continued buying Venezuelan energy.By late March, new tariffs on lumber, microchips, pharma, and copper were planned for later in April, as part of a rolling pressure campaign, though specific rates were not yet implemented. These were justified as efforts to protect American industries deemed critical or to retaliate for what the U.S. views as unfair advantages enjoyed by foreign producers (for instance, the administration hinted at curbing semiconductor imports to press EU and Asian allies to align on tech policies).April tariffs: the goal is a ceasefire and to defeat stagflationThe administration signaled it would impose a flat, double-digit (20â€“25%) tariff on all imports from the European Union, aiming for a rate roughly matching the tariffs and barriers the EU applies to U.S. exports. This unprecedented moveâ€”treating the entire EU as a single target in a trade warâ€”was justified by the White House as enforcing â€œfair reciprocity,â€ arguing that Europeâ€™s own import tariffs (such as the EUâ€™s standard 10% tariff on cars, agricultural duties, and various non-tariff barriers like regulations) have disadvantaged U.S. exporters for years.As noted, the 25% auto tariff was announced in late March and set to take effect on April 3. By April, the impact on European carmakers was expected to be significant, with essentially all passenger vehicle exports to the U.S. facing a steep levy. Europeâ€™s auto industry is a major exporter (the EU shipped about â‚¬37 billion in cars to the U.S. in 2022), so this tariff directly hits a substantial flow of trade.Following steel, aluminum, and autos, the Trump trade team indicated that new tariffs on lumber, semiconductors, pharmaceuticals, and possibly chemicals would be rolled out in phases throughout April. For instance, duties on imported microchips and drug products were on the agenda (impacting European tech and pharma firms). These measures were part of Trumpâ€™s strategy of incremental escalationâ€”using the threat of further pain to extract concessions in negotiations. By mid- to late April, however, the expectation in Europe was that no major industry would be spared if talks failed; the U.S. was prepared to tax a broad array of EU goods, effectively weaponizing Americaâ€™s entire import market as leverage.These tariffs come at a time when the European economy is already grappling with post-pandemic recovery and external shocks (like high energy prices stemming from the Ukraine conflict). Additional tariffs act like a tax on trade, potentially raising costs and prices (inflationary) while dampening demand (recessionary). The European Commission has voiced concerns that tit-for-tat tariff wars could â€œreignite global inflationâ€ and undercut growth. The EU must protect its economic interests and respond to U.S. trade aggression, but without hurting itself further.The Trump administration has justified its EU-directed tariffs as a necessary correction to perceived inequities (making the EU pay a price for its trade surplus and higher tariffs), a means to safeguard key U.S. industries and interests, and a leverage tool to negotiate better terms. But the key is that these tariffs are tools to force the EU to the negotiating table, compelling it to accept the upcoming â€œHoly Alliance 2.0â€ peace proposal. As I wrote, Trump needs to resolve this and eliminate Western stagflation by Easter. According to the original plans, a ceasefire should be in place by Q3.What can the EU do? In late 2023, the EU passed the Anti-Coercion Instrument, a new legal tool to respond to economic intimidation by non-EU countries. This was designed precisely for situations where a partner uses trade measures to coerce the EU or its members (the law was partly inspired by incidents such as Chinaâ€™s trade blockade of Lithuania). The ACI allows the EU to swiftly implement retaliatory measures beyond tariffsâ€”including restrictions on services, investments, or procurementâ€”if it determines that a foreign country is trying to coerce the EU. Trumpâ€™s blanket tariffs and explicit bargaining approach (â€œdo X or face tariffsâ€) could well be deemed economic coercion under the ACIâ€™s definition. For example, under the ACI, the EU could ban U.S. companies from participating in certain EU public tenders or restrict exports of critical EU goods to the U.S., as a targeted response.The EU can coordinate its response with other nations also targeted by U.S. tariffs. In 2025, the Trump tariffs are hitting allies like China, Canada, Mexico, Japan, BRICS, and others simultaneously. For instance, the EU, Canada, and Mexico could jointly refuse to negotiate under tariff blackmail and perhaps collectively threaten action through forums like the G7 or G20.The EU can challenge the U.S. tariffs through the World Trade Organizationâ€™s dispute mechanism. In fact, after the 2018 steel tariffs, the EU (and others) brought cases against the U.S. at the WTO. A formal legal challenge claims that the U.S. measures violate WTO rules (for example, the most-favored-nation principle and agreed tariff bindings). While the WTO process is slow and currently hampered (the Appellate Body is not fully functional due to U.S. blockages), pursuing a case still has value. It signals the EUâ€™s principled stance and might eventually authorize the EU to impose WTO-sanctioned counter-tariffs if it wins (essentially legal retaliation).Also, the European Central Bank can play a crucial role in buffering the EU economy against trade-war shocks. If U.S. tariffs begin to bite, the ECB could loosen monetary policyâ€”for instance, by delaying planned interest rate hikes or even cutting rates/stimulus if growth falters. By keeping credit cheap, the ECB would support European businesses through a rough patch and help counteract any rise in borrowing costs caused by market jitters. An ancillary effect of easier ECB policy is a weaker euro, which helps EU exporters compete internationally (partly offsetting U.S. tariffs, as a cheaper euro makes European goods less expensive in dollar terms). Indeed, in response to the tariffs and the â€œmoney goes homeâ€ capital shift, the euro has depreciated somewhatâ€”privately welcomed by EU officials as it boosts export competitiveness. They will let the exchange rate adjust naturally as a shock absorber. The ECB will also be ready to provide liquidity to financial markets if necessary (through asset purchases or swap lines) to prevent a credit crunch in Europe if U.S. actions cause severe market dislocation. Essentially, monetary policy is a tool to stabilize confidence: as the U.S. raises import barriers, the ECB can reassure firms that financing will remain available and that policymakers are supporting demand. This mitigates recession risk. (One must note, however, that the ECBâ€™s primary mandate is controlling inflation. If tariffs drive inflation up via higher import prices, the ECB faces a balancing act. Yet given the priority of maintaining growth and financial stability, it will likely err on the side of accommodation, as President Lagarde has indicated openness to looking through one-off price impacts in favor of the broader outlook.)On the fiscal side, the EU (and member state governments) can deploy spending and subsidies to blunt the tariffsâ€™ impact. The European Commission could temporarily relax its strict budget rules, allowing member countries to run slightly higher deficits to fund relief measures.In an extreme scenario where capital flight from the EU becomes destabilizing (for instance, if U.S. companies begin divesting en masse or if the dollar soars in value at the euroâ€™s expense), the EU could consider measures to manage these flows. Full capital controls are unlikely in a free-market EU, but heightened investment screening might prevent opportunistic takeovers of strategic European firms if their valuations drop. EU regulators could also coordinate to discourage hasty withdrawalsâ€”for example, by diplomatically urging U.S. multinational companies not to cut and run from European operations, or by highlighting the long-term costs of leaving. The EU could seek support from other global investors (such as sovereign wealth funds from friendly countries) to step in and invest in Europe, offsetting any U.S. pullout. The key is to ensure that if â€œsome money goes homeâ€ to America, other money comes into Europe from elsewhere. By maintaining an attractive investment climateâ€”i.e., not retaliating in ways that scare off all foreign investorsâ€”the EU can mitigate the financial impact.On the defensive side, the EU can adjust its import sources. Higher U.S. prices (whether due to tariffs or any EU response) mean Europe can replace some U.S. imports with domestic or alternative foreign suppliers. The EU had already implemented steel import safeguards in response to Trumpâ€™s steel tariffsâ€”basically limiting imports from elsewhere so that steel diverted from the U.S. would not flood Europe. It could tighten these quotas further to protect EU steelmakers who are now hurt on the other side by U.S. tariffs. For agricultural commodities, if the EU imposes countermeasures not as tariffs but rather as quotas or restrictions, it can manage supply. Additionally, reducing dependence on U.S. high-tech inputs (such as certain semiconductors or software) is already a strategic goal for Europe; the trade war merely reinforces the drive for tech sovereignty (e.g., investing in European chip fabs so that U.S. export restrictions or tariffs on chips are less harmful).On the offensive side, the EU could speed up negotiations on trade deals with willing partners. For example, finalizing pending EU trade agreements with countries like Australia, India, or the Mercosur bloc (Brazil/Argentina, etc.) could open new markets for European goods. If American protectionism closes one door, opening others is crucial.And the most important thing: China and the EU could directly strike at U.S. economic interests. U.S. Treasuries are viewed as the benchmark for global financial stability. If the EU sold off a significant portion of its holdings, it could depress bond prices and push up interest rates. This sudden shift could unsettle global financial markets, leading to higher volatility and elevated borrowing costsâ€”not only in the U.S. but worldwide. Thatâ€™s why we saw a small increase in CDS prices when the PCE figures came out: The timing is very telling. Look at how much it has risen in relative terms over the last few weeks.Such an aggressive move would definitely hit the EU as well, since it would destabilize global markets, and the EU canâ€™t reposition itself that quickly (and I doubt itâ€™s intended to). However, it is temporarily financing military resources and commodity stockpiles for its reserves.In a bipolar, fragmented world, commodity prices become far more sensitive to various external shocks than in a globally cooperative, integrated world. Furthermore, as countries migrate from one bloc to another, exchange rate dynamics change. Restricting free trade leads to a smaller economic surplus. I recommend reading the IMFâ€™s 2023 paper on re-polarization.Sell-off, bottomWhat weâ€™re seeing now is that despite Fridayâ€™s events, the market seems complacent. Itâ€™s not taking the trade war seriously, which is quietly and gradually unfolding under the hood. I would say, around last year April levels.JPM Collar expiryBefore we move on to the weekly skew, I need to mention the JPM Collar expiration on Monday. In last weekâ€™s weekly post, I already explained how the collar works in detail. Itâ€™s very important to understand this, because thereâ€™s a lot of misinformation about it on social media.The collar primarily affects the vega profile in the weeks leading up to expiration. Its impact is especially significant near the short call strike and below the long put strike at expiration, due to gamma.As I wrote, they let the collar expireâ€”they donâ€™t close it. Meanwhile, around 2 PM, they open a new collar, which they delta-neutralize with a 0DTE call to remove the extra synthetic short exposure the double collar would otherwise create during the final two hours.If the spot is above the long put strike (5565), there can be only a mild supportive effect. The OTM expiring put spread provides minor support, which the new collar largely absorbs. However, if the put spread expires ITM, then short vanna, long charm, and short speed create a suppressive environment toward the lower leg (4700), roughly towards 5114.24The collarâ€™s psychological impact is bigger than its actual influence on flows. These dynamics are already underway and will continue to play out, as many have written and as an increasing number are now writing, and they will set the tone for the period ahead.In 2025, in addition to corporate earnings, the marketâ€™s main engines were valuation expansion and fiscal and monetary stimulus, so a buy-the-dip approach broadly generated net positive returns. However, a number of challenges and risks have accumulated for the year ahead.In 2026, we should expect real interest rates to rise as inflation expectations fall at an accelerating pace, creating a drag on growth. And if labour-market trends continue, the liquidity support from further rate cuts will not necessarily offset a weakening macro backdrop. I think there is a strong likelihood that the Fed will pause its rate-cutting cycle in the first quarter of 2026, and then resume easing in June after a new chair takes office. (I would add that, relative to gold, the European equity index has outperformed U.S. equities by 23%.) Over the past year, long-duration U.S. Treasuries have generated a âˆ’33.33% return in gold terms.Record-low credit risk premia (tight credit spreads) suggest that expected equity returns will be low going forward.Corporate bond spreads have also tightened to record lows, which previously benefited both lower-quality bonds and equities, but by now there is not much room left for further spread compression. Overall, there is little scope for additional excess return from equity risk premia, credit spreads, and liquidity premia.And if Bessent fails to keep bond yields low and demand continues to weaken, yields may riseâ€”which, with todayâ€™s overvalued asset prices, could trigger a very deep correction, potentially even a bear market.SubscribeReversion model flags 6,889.84 as the level that must hold for the rally to extend. There is no excessive short-term momentum on the horizon.On a multi-year basis, however, the model shows a 92.81% probability of a mean reversion below 6,000. In backtests, I typically need 95%+ to call a high-conviction topping signal, but this reading already helps explain why investors are in â€œsell-into-strengthâ€ mode.(These signals appeared in 2019, 2020, 2022, and again in 2025 ahead of the April crash)The market, as it stands today, is no longer suitable for investment: rotation is underway, while significant stagflation, credit, geopolitical, and social risks are accumulating.The problems are far deeper and longer-term than many believe. As I wrote several times in March and April, the reshaping of the geopolitical structure as we have known it has begun; the United States must fight to preserve its hegemonic role while also solving the challenges of a changing global economic order. These struggles are intensifying.The market is currently dominated by hedge funds and private multi-billionaire speculators, such as Soros, who know how to pocket as much as possible from an inflating bubble and from rising vomma risk before the bubble bursts.The market favors momentum traders and speculators. Let me show you these two charts:andSwap spread is the fixed rate you receive in an interestâ€‘rate swap minus the yield on the corresponding Treasury (e.g. 30â€‘year swap rate minus 30â€‘year UST). When spreads turn negative, it means swap dealers are willing to pay fixed at a rate below what the U.S. government offersâ€”because they desperately want to receive floating SOFR or obtain Treasury collateral.Last print for 30y is â€“93â€¯bp, well below its longâ€‘run average around â€“80â€¯bp, and near its worst (â€“97â€¯bp on Aprilâ€¯8). Short tenors (2Y at â€“24â€¯bp) are â€œonlyâ€ modestly negative; the long end (20Y, 30Y) is far more inverted. This tells that stress is concentrated in longerâ€‘dated funding. (see my â€˜hidden QEâ€™ call) This signals signals severe funding and liquidity stress: banks would rather pay to receive floating (SOFR) than hold Treasuries, reflecting concerns over collateral scarcity, repo market strains and heightened credit risk.Historically, extreme negative swap spreads have preceded bondâ€‘market meltdowns (2008, 2020â€¦), foreshadowed equity drawdowns, as funding strains propagate to riskâ€‘assets via leveraged hedge funds and deltaâ€‘hedged positions and force central banks to adjust regulatory ratios (e.g., SLR waivers) or deploy liquidity backstops (standing repo, QE) to restore market functioning.It is the weighing machine, not the voting one.SubscribeThe key is again the timingâ€¦ Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

Market analysisVIX downside vomma 'risk', remember? + Intraday post (03/July)Jul 03, 2025âˆ™ Paid111ShareGood morning to everyone who loves the whiff of that fresh-mattered morning NFP print.Yesterday I posted a new educational article about my trading principles covering both practical applications and principiums. Take your time to read and re-read it. Algos are set to take a recessionary print as positive-to-neutral today, while an inflationary print would be taken more like flat. Btw, last night, I digged into tariffs and datas a bit more... Tariffs act like some sales taxes on imports, raising prices at the checkout, because importers pass the duty on to consumers. In this economic environment nominal income growth underpins nominal spending growth, even tho credit growth remains dampened (DPI +0.8%, PCE +0.2% - illustrating how income gains still support spending; Consumer Credit +4.3% - below past credit-fuelled cycles).The 90 days pause led to a surge in arrivals at US ports roughly three weeks after its announcement, basically pulling forward demand masking the fact that core income dynamic still underpins the cycle. Now, core macro theory holds that when nominal incomes lag price rises, households either draw down savings, borrow more, or cut real spending. This would cause GDP growth slowdown and profit margin compression. Trump's work-visa rescissions and ICE raids already tightened the labor market. Basically any drop in foreign-born labor keeps the unemployment rate artificially low and bolsters wages, regardless of tariff policy. So it generates a wage inflation as firms are forced to pay more for scarce labor. And this is independent from the tariffs! This is crucial. Plus, across used cars, hotels/STRs, raw materials, housing, restaurants, and wages, we can see demand-pull pressures as too much demand chasing too few goods.And if I'm right, then stagflation is actually worsening and the Fed's â€œneutralâ€ rate has likely shifted higher. This will tighten Fed's room again to cut rates, and this is why I see growth concerns ('bad news are bad news' regime) looming back through bonds vs equities.I want you to remember something I showed you in the weekly postâ€¦ Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

Any pathâ€”cutting or holding ratesâ€”risks long-term damage to the Fedâ€™s credibility and independence. Cutting rates under political duress may produce short-term gains yet compromise the Fedâ€™s apolitical mandate, while holding steady could invite harsher political attacks if growth slows.The broader FOMCâ€”including the voting regional presidentsâ€”remains cautious, implying the Fed may delay action until clearer data emerge on inflation and growth. # Vomma supply, Short vanna, Risk - Intraday post (29/Aug)

> Weekly post:

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/vomma-supply-short-vanna-risk-intraday

---

Market analysisVomma supply, Short vanna, Risk - Intraday post (29/Aug)Aug 29, 2025âˆ™ Paid11ShareWeekly post: Market analysisWeekly post (24-29/Aug)AlmaÂ·August 24, 2025Last week I said that any vol spike keeps being managed through vomma supply during this whole month, and any directional momentum must be confirmed, because everything is uncertain again.Read full story(Yesterday post was free for everybody)Here I wrote about PCE: NVDA ER, GDP, PCE, Valuation - Intraday post (27/Aug)AlmaÂ·August 27, 2025Read full storyVomma supply is on (grey line), and very heavy, as we keep seeing it day by day, week by week: especially on the right-tail, because spot/vol beta is about 21.01% with an avg of 16.24%: The reason is illiquidity and short squeeze. I also note here that 3m realized volatility is at 9.38% while 1m-RV is at 10.11%, while realized vvol is sold from 81.3% to 26.59% in only one week! This is an agressive â€œhigher for longerâ€ vol environment making quant funds to scale out of long exposures, decreasing insitutional buying flow in terms of investment purpose (because they buyback hedges, levered up through MM buybacks as well, as put spreads etc. are closed).This means that the current SPX levels are not stable at all, and will be held until Sept cut, simultanously compressing risk premium. In the weekly post, I already elaborated on the reason of this. This is a cautious â€œstairstep-upâ€/â€elevator upâ€ kind of scenario, while funds cautiously keep upside squeezes in check as well. Through vomma supply, concentrated on the right-tail of the distribution, they create a â€œbrakingâ€ effect, in order to extend this melt-up momentum. With this, long vol/long delta strategies canâ€™t really capitalize on this move, while locking the cheapness of the downside hedges, vvariances for the future correction. The secondary effect is cooling down quant algos that take the velocity of the momentum into account, so they meassure lower risk.  If volatility would be sold, market would not find enough liquidity at these levels. Simply because, market is about supply and demand balance. If customers sell vanna, they bet on negative spot/vol correlation (simply because the delta vs. volatility relationship is inverse in this position). Now, firstly it opens up the left-tail; secondly, when spot goes through the centroid of the vanna and speed profile to the upside, the imbalance shifts to the sell-side. In order to realize the distribution bet, market needs customers who buy all these sold assets at those prices. And this selling flow is increasing as spot is climbing upward. It is clear now that there is no such high buying liquidity here that could swallow all that potential selling, because there is no faith, no fundamental support for that. Funds are scaling out to cash and safe-havens, and retails are highly volatile traders. However, through buying vanna from dealers, levering-up the upside squeeze, once can amplify the move, but also braking it simultanously by flattening the convexity through vomma selling. And as said, the ultimate benefit is cheap hedges against vol squeezes in the futureâ€¦ Donâ€™t forget: itâ€™s not about inflation, itâ€™s about the growing debt burden!This is what Trump is aiming to manage. And this is why he slowly gains full control on Fed and othersâ€¦Also, we will see EOM rebalancing from investors who reduce their stock exposures due to the high risk, and buying bonds and safe havens. The reason is the high stock prices, that increases the weight of stocks in the portfolio against low risk assets. It caps upside. **Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/weekly-post-17-21march

Market analysisWeekly post (17-21/march) Mar 17, 2025âˆ™ Paid2822ShareIn this post I explained the geo context, that is crucial important bcs in these times where the world order is about to shift, the geopolitics, economy and market are breathing together.Here I explained the spot decay again and touched this OpEx - EOM window dynamics with JPM collar vs OpEx.I want to add, that on multiple occasions, the 1987 Washington Agreement between the Soviet Union and the USA has been mentionedâ€”a deal in which both sides pledged to dismantle their ground-launched intermediate and shorter-range missiles. In 2019, Donald Trump was the first to withdraw from this agreement, followed by Vladimir Putin the very next day. Yet both parties continue to indicate willingness to abide by its terms on the condition that they can conduct official inspections at each otherâ€™s missile bases at any time. Of course, no such inspections have taken place officially since then.On July 8, 1997 when NATO invited Hungary, Poland, and the Czech Republic to join, Russia and NATO signed the Founding Act, in which Russia accepted NATOâ€™s future eastern expansions. In return, NATO promised not to deploy nuclear weapons within these new member states and, barring national security needs, not to station any further military forces in those countries. NATO fully adhered to this agreement until 2014, and even at the outbreak of the Crimean conflicts, it only stationed a symbolic number of troops along its border.Furthermore, last December, Russia presented the USA and NATO with a near-ultimatum in the form of a draft treaty. They demanded: 1) the former Soviet states that are not yet NATO members must never be allowed to join NATO; 2) NATO must not deploy strike capabilities, either near the Russian border or within Ukraine, and 3) NATO must roll back its infrastructure to its pre-1997 status.NATO rejected points 1 and 3, but was willing to negotiate on point 2â€”however, those negotiations have yet to take place.The issue, therefore, runs far deeper than what the media suggests. Neither side is beyond reproach, but it was NATO that began to violate agreements with an aggressive approach towards Russian borders, while Russiaâ€”whose identity inherently involves feeling perpetually threatenedâ€”took preemptive steps against NATO.This is the problem that needs solution. As I always say, a peace reached too quickly is no real peace, and a partial ceasefire is no real ceasefire. Progress is slow, but itâ€™s happening. Fortunately, thereâ€™s no rush among the guys in Jeddah, and as far as I hear, negotiations are heading in a good directionâ€”just inch by inch. # Weekly post (24-28/march)

> Bias into EOM, JPM collar

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/weekly-post-24-28march

---

Market analysisWeekly post (24-28/march)Bias into EOM, JPM collarMar 23, 2025âˆ™ Paid55413ShareThe essence of Friday's supportive OpEx laid, on one hand, in the fact that after the AM expo, dealers had to buy back a significant amount of delta, as I had signaled before the opening. On the other hand, the local positioning was long vanna, short charm, and long color, which reduced the negative gamma effect as the day progressed. In addition, the overall positioning was neutral-to-suppressive, with almost zero negative speed exposure, which, along with declining vol, compressed the delta exposure during the day and thus prevented any rally whatsoever. But most importantly, it was the algorithms operating in HF mean reversion trading and long dispersion mode, which I have been continuously signaling for dynamism over the past weeks, whose natural consequence was the suppression of the range of daily returnsâ€”and consequently, of volatility. This was supportive in the local long vanna environment.And why does oscillation occur, why is there a need for low volatility? Read this and this post, or this one.Now that we're past OpEx, we need to determine what the market wants until the end-of-month expiry. And this is where the JPM collar will come into effect. So now I write a bit about it again in advance.JPM Put Spread CollarJPM hedge equity operation actually consists of three funds, with JHEQX being the largest. These funds operate continuously and have overlapping expirations.Unlike a traditional protective collar trade, this fund uses a debit spread. The extra premium gained from selling the put option lets the manager raise the call strike and increase the cap. This spread protects against losses beyond roughly 5%, but after about 20% losses, the protection ends and performance tracks the market oneâ€‘toâ€‘one. It does not offer coverage against extreme tail events. Also they use SPXW options (EOM, PMâ€‘settled), so execution and resetting are tied to the closing settlement.Once the collar is established around 2 PM (the official settlement), the fund managers do not actively adjust or roll the position. This is a common misconception. Instead, the collar simply expires, and they reset it with a new one. Thereâ€™s no active monetization of the collar during its lifetime. Since the new collar adds further short delta exposure, they also open a 0DTE call leg to neutralize that delta.See the official brochure here.Here you can see the 4700/5565/6165 collar on EOM expo:Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

This is called risk parity. The lower the volatility goes, the higher its weight becomes in the portfolio, resulting increased buying activity. # What is volatility?

> Math warning!

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/what-is-volatility

---

Educational postsWhat is volatility?Math warning!Jan 25, 2025âˆ™ Paid611410Share In this article, I'm going to introduce the concept of volatility, log return, and standard deviation, providing the mathematical background to help you gain a better understanding of these concepts. I will also introduce the four main volatility formulas to illustrate the evolution of volatility estimation: Parkinson, Garman-Klass, Roger-Satchell, and Yang-Zhang.Math warning!When we talk about volatility, we look at the returns rather than closing prices because returns reveal the actual magnitude and direction of the daily price changes, standardizing the measurement of price variability across time and different assets.By definition, volatility is the annualized standard deviation of the periodic logarithmic returns. And like this volatility basically shows us the â€œreal velocityâ€ of the price action indirectly giving us a picture about the liquidity at certain spot levels. Liquidity means how easy it is to turn an asset back into cash. The higher the liquidity, the easier and more cost efficient it is to do, and vice versa. if volatility is low, this means that the log returns are compressing as a result of high buying and selling activity at certain territories, because the interest are met there. Au contrary, when volatility is high, this means that the buyers and sellers must â€œlook for each otherâ€, and so the price movements expand, so does volatility.But now, letâ€™s dissect this definition of log returns starting with math basicsâ€¦What is logarithmic return?Return is the profit on a trade which the trader receives from that position over a given time period.Letâ€™s say Váµ¢ is the initial value of our capital and the end value is Vâ‚‘Then simple return is:Return=Veâˆ’ViViContinue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.Next

# What is vomma supply? Why is it important? - Intraday post (04/Sept)

> As everybody already knows that the coming Fed cut will be a hawkish cut due to the checkmate situation, volatility keeps being managed for this week and day to day.

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/what-is-vomma-supply-why-is-it-important

---

Market analysisWhat is vomma supply? Why is it important? - Intraday post (04/Sept)Sep 04, 2025âˆ™ Paid161ShareAs everybody already knows that the coming Fed cut will be a hawkish cut due to the checkmate situation, volatility keeps being managed for this week and day to day.Vomma selling is basically like selling VIX calls or selling tail SPX OTM puts/calls for the dealers and bidding up gamma at the tails. It doesnâ€™t prevent wider intraday swings, it lets the spot moves kinda freely in a relatively wide range, but it prevents vol spikes, it dampens any upsticks, by compressing the kurtosis of the distribution. This is a less agressive strategy than constantly selling volatility, dumping it down to hell, like it was done in 2022. Those setups are like an extended rubber band, they are very sensitive to any vol spikes, leading into a vol squeeze.For a vomma squeeze, market needs extreme tail event. But we need to know where the â€œevent horizonz" of the tailâ€ lies. Thats something I built formulas for as well. So vomma supply doesnâ€™t mean that volatility is suppressed. It only means that, the more vol goes up, the more vol exposure the dealers get, so they will sell volatility. (think like this: long vomma = vol up/vega up = selling vega by dealers to hedge)Or think about it like supplying the dealers with VIX upside gamma. And because VIX smirk is mostly concave (especially for longer maturities), but have an upward skew for sure (it is because of the modelled mean reverting tendency of volatility), when VIX spikes, dealers will have a lot to delta hedge (and maybe VIX-vega hedge as well, but itâ€™s conditional), capping the vol spike, and creating a VIX-to-SPX lead-lag. This is because VIX is the 30-day variance object derived from SPX options, so spot moves change SPX option vol premiums and so thus the implied variance curve (think vanna/cross-gamma), which moves VIX futures. To reduce this additional exposure, dealers add an ES hedge sized by the VXâ€“ES beta. The result is buying ES futures at the tail strikes, dampening or even braking the spot move. It can work both ways, as I keep telling. Because spot/vol correlation can be both positive and negative.The third thing is to simply sell tail SPX puts, that are basically synthetic VIX calls. You can see a lot of this on these intraday setups, OptionsDepth Heatmapsâ€¦ especially to the upside, because in the recent times, intraday setups and even weekly/monthly setups where kept being right-tail heavy. And so, by creating positive gammas to both upside and downside, it simply builds heavy gamma walls to the spot that swallow vol spike-driven spot moves, by leveraging dealer hedging flow. The net result is a compressed kurtosis for SPX distribution. Thinner tails, reduced tail-risks to both direction, and controlled volatility swings. SubscribePlaying with inflation swaps or any assets that fits the narrative is only a metaphorical â€œvomma supplyâ€ as it keeps sentiment in check. For example, now, the short-term SPY/yields correlation is dumped again. This makes algos and retails to buy every dip on weak datas as those datas will trigger Fed cut (â€œbad news are god newsâ€). Okay, so now letâ€™s jump into todayâ€™s positioningâ€¦ Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# "Who's bluffing?" USA vs. China poker game + Intraday post (10/April)

> Digging into the April 9 events

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/whos-bluffing-usa-vs-china-poker

---

Market analysis"Who's bluffing?" USA vs. China poker game + Intraday post (10/April)Digging into the April 9 eventsApr 10, 2025âˆ™ Paid2622ShareHere comes the continuation of yesterdayâ€™s postâ€¦Iâ€™ll explain what the title refers to. If youâ€™re playing poker against a strong opponent and the stakes keep rising relentlessly, and youâ€™re no longer sure how to read your opponentâ€™s reactionsâ€”how far theyâ€™re willing to goâ€”you might pull off a double feint, which means pretending temporal weakness and watching how your opponent responds. If they also reduce the pressure, thereâ€™s a pretty good chance theyâ€™re just bluffing.However, weâ€™re observing the game from the outside, trying to figure out which player to bet on. Because right now, weâ€™re speculators, not investors. The question, then, is: whoâ€™s bluffing: US or China?Iâ€™m going to explore that topic now, in light of yesterdayâ€™s ~10% run-up.The April 9 twistTrumpâ€™s April 9 tariff policy twist â€“ elevating tariffs on China (especially consumer goods) while delaying or reducing tariffs on other countries â€“ was a deliberate strategic pivot. To understand its implications, we should break down the policy and economic logic.By 2025, China was by far the largest source of U.S. consumer product imports (everything from electronics to apparel to appliances). In prior tariff rounds (2018â€“2019), the Trump administration had often exempted many consumer goods or imposed lower rates, partly to avoid direct pain on U.S. consumers. This time, Trump went all-in, slapping tariffs that reportedly exceeded 100% on a broad swath of Chinese exports. The rationale seemed to be punitive â€“ to pressure China intensely â€“ but it also means U.S. importers and consumers will face sharp price increases on everyday goods. By contrast, tariffs on key allies (Europe, Canada, Japan, etc.) were paused at 10% or avoided entirely for 90 days, giving those countries a chance to negotiate or adjust supply chains.Trumpâ€™s likely calculation was that he could afford to anger China but did not want to completely alienate U.S. allies and U.S. businesses all at once. Itâ€™s a divide-and-conquer approach in the trade war. Crucial.Tariffs on consumer goods are generally inflationary in the short run â€“ they act as a sales tax on imports, raising prices for U.S. buyers. For example, Federal Reserve economists have estimated that a broad 10% tariff on imports could lift core CPI inflation by a few tenths of a percent over the course of a year. In this case, with over $500 billion of Chinese goods facing tariffs potentially above 50-100%, U.S. consumer price indices will see upward pressure as those costs filter through. Indeed, Fed Governor Adriana Kugler noted in early April that some of the recent rise in goods inflation was â€œanticipatoryâ€ of the tariffsâ€™ effect, as businesses and consumers rushed to buy items (like cars and appliances) before higher prices hit.However, while tariffs can create price spikes, they also weaken demand, which can be deflationary down the line. Consumers confronted with significantly higher prices may cut back spending or switch to cheaper alternatives. If real incomes donâ€™t keep up, overall consumption falls. Businesses, seeing higher input costs and potentially lower sales volumes, might reduce investment or hiring. In the broader sense, tariffs act like a tax increase on the economy â€“ sucking purchasing power and potentially leading to demand destruction. Yesterday I wrote that Powell articulated this dilemma: trade tariffs simultaneously slow growth and raise inflation in the short term. The Fed and others feared a stagflationary hit, where the Fedâ€™s usual policy response is complicated (easing helps growth but could stoke inflation, whereas tightening would curb inflation but deepen the slowdown). If policy errs on the side of tightness (to fight tariff-driven inflation), a deeper slump and even deflationary spiral could result once the one-off price boosts fade. By delaying or lowering tariffs on U.S. allies, Trump likely hoped to ease global opposition and cushion the impact on supply chains. Europe, for instance, was given a 90-day reprieve, which European officials welcomed as â€œgood newsâ€ enabling further talks. This means supply routes from Europe, Latin America, and other partners could temporarily substitute for some Chinese imports (where feasible) without incurring tariffs â€“ an intentional pressure tactic against Beijing.However, if companies shift sourcing to avoid Chinese tariffs, it could be less efficient or more costly, still raising prices somewhat. Moreover, this selective approach could distort trade flows: countries not subject to the tariff (say Vietnam or Mexico) might see a surge in demand for their goods, potentially causing bottlenecks or higher prices there. In effect, while the tariff delay for others helps avoid a synchronized price shock on all imports, it doesnâ€™t fully eliminate inflationary pressure â€“ it merely concentrates it on China-exposed supply chains. It also means China bears the brunt of the economic hit among U.S. trade partners, which was precisely Trumpâ€™s intent.By sharply raising import costs on consumer goods, the tariffs could indeed reduce real consumer spending â€“ households might delay purchases or downtrade to cheaper goods, slowing economic activity. If businesses canâ€™t pass on all costs, some may cut margins or lay off workers, adding slack to the labor market. Already, by March 2025 there were signs of disinflation: the Fed noted that progress toward its 2% inflation target had stalled and that underlying inflation measures were not falling. If the trade war induces a recession, the likely trajectory would be an initial bump in inflation (from tariffs) followed by a downturn where demand collapses and prices start to fall. We can look at China as a cautionary example: China entered deflation this year partly because exports weakened amid trade tensions. In March, Chinaâ€™s CPI was down 0.1% year-on-year (a second monthly decline) and producer prices were down 2.5%, with analysts explicitly citing the escalating U.S. trade war and unsold exports as factors driving Chinese prices lower. In effect, China is exporting deflation â€“ its excess supply and lower-cost goods, facing tariffs, either pile up domestically (forcing price cuts) or get diverted globally, pushing prices down elsewhere. For the U.S., tariffs on consumer goods from China mean Americans pay more at the store, unless Chinese firms cut their prices to retain market share.There is some evidence Chinese exporters may have to absorb part of the tariff by lowering prices (to remain competitive), which ironically reinforces deflationary pressure in China and globally. If Chinese firms slash prices by, say, 20% to offset a 100% tariff, U.S. consumers see a 80% net price rise on those goods, but Chinese profit margins collapse â€“ leading potentially to factory layoffs and further deflation in China. That, in turn, can spill back to the U.S. through cheaper intermediate goods or a weaker Chinese yuan.If Trump intended the dramatic tariff announcement followed by a quick partial retreat as a grand bluff to rattle China but then reassure Wall Street, it partially succeeded â€“ he demonstrated he was â€œcrazy enoughâ€ to go nuclear on tariffs, but also that he would blink if markets melted down too far. This could strengthen his hand diplomatically (showing China that the U.S. has emergency mechanisms to buffer its markets, so China shouldnâ€™t assume the U.S. will cave easily). However, itâ€™s a dangerous game: relying on the Fed and markets to cushion self-inflicted shocks undermine long-term confidence from the Middle-Eastâ€™s and Russiaâ€™s part. When the worldâ€™s safest asset (U.S. Treasuries) suddenly behaves unpredictably, it rattles global trust. If big holders like Saudi Arabiaâ€™s SAMA, UAEâ€™s ADIA, or Russiaâ€™s sovereign fund see wild swings and the prospect of U.S. policy driving down the value of their holdings, they may reconsider allocations.China US debt sellingIf China were to retaliate by aggressively selling U.S. Treasuries and ramping up tariffs, the near-term effect would be sharply higher U.S. interest rates, severe stress in financial markets, and a further collapse in bilateral trade. Also Europeâ€™s economy, already stagnating, could tip into deep recession as global trade falls and financial uncertainty spikes.Even the fear of Chinese selling itself is enough to move the market: recall that in early April, â€œmarkets [were] concerned that China and other countries could â€˜dumpâ€™ U.S. Treasuries as a retaliation tool. Hence, UST yields [went] up.â€ We saw yields leap to multi-year highs partly on this concern. If actual dumping occurred, a disorderly surge in yields could result. For example, the 10-year yield could shoot well above 5% or 6% in short order if thereâ€™s no intervention. Such a spike raises borrowing costs across the board â€“ mortgages, car loans, business loans, and government debt â€“ tightening financial conditions dramatically.Highly leveraged funds would face margin calls as bond prices fell, potentially causing some to fail. The U.S. government itself would face sharply higher interest costs; if sustained, this would balloon the federal deficit due to higher debt service, creating a vicious cycle of more borrowing and further upward pressure on yields. In extreme scenarios, this can lead to a self-reinforcing selloff or even questions about U.S. debt sustainability as investors lose confidence. While the U.S. could ultimately rely on the Fed to buy its debt (more on that soon), the interim volatility would be severe.The Federal Reserveâ€™s likely reaction to a massive foreign selloff would be to intervene as a buyer of last resort. This means launching a new round of quantitative easing, explicitly purchasing Treasuries to absorb the supply China (and potentially others) dump. In effect, the Fed would be monetizing the debt to keep yields from spiraling upward. The Fed could also cut interest rates (if not already at zero) to offset tightening. However, these actions have consequences: rapidly expanding the Fedâ€™s balance sheet to counteract a foreign selloff might undermine confidence in the dollar or future inflation. It could be seen as the Fed accommodating fiscal needs (debt financing), potentially denting its inflation-fighting credibility. Yet, not acting is likely worse â€“ if yields spiked uncontrollably, the U.S. economy would crash. So the Fed would almost surely step in, as Bank of England did in 2022 when UK bond yields surged. The question is whether the Fed could buy fast enough to stabilize the market and whether doing so would introduce longer-term inflation risk. This essentially becomes a contest between Chinaâ€™s selling and the Fedâ€™s buying. The Fed, with infinite printing power, can in theory always win, but at the possible cost of devaluing the currency.Howeverâ€¦Selling Treasuries would also impact Chinaâ€™s economy. If it causes the yuan to rise (because selling USD assets tends to push USD down vs yuan), Chinese exports become less competitive at a time they are already hampered by tariffs. Chinaâ€™s central bank could counter by easing monetary policy â€“ cutting interest rates or reserve requirements to stimulate domestic demand â€“ trying to offset export losses with internal growth. They could also impose stricter capital controls to prevent their own firms from taking money out during the turmoil. Additionally, China could accelerate trade with other partners (the EU, ASEAN) to make up for lost U.S. business, although global recession would make that hard. If the confrontation is that severe, China might accept a couple of years of very slow growth or recession as a cost of standing up to the U.S., just as the U.S. would be enduring a recession too. Essentially, itâ€™d be a test of whose system can absorb more pain.(Chinese authorities have more direct control over their economy (state banks, state-owned enterprises, etc.) and could probably maintain stability (e.g., forbid layoffs or use fiscal firepower to keep things running) for some time even in a downturn. Politically, they would cast it as resisting U.S. aggression, which might rally nationalist support despite economic hardship.)The April 9 SpikeBRICS and Middle-East resources say that the dramatic intraday rebound did not represent a healthy rebalancing but rather a â€œcircuit breakerâ€ response engineered by the Fed. They note that while the U.S. ultimately averted a collapse, the market reaction exposed vulnerabilities that could be exploited if shocks recur. Although they emphasize that the U.S. remains fundamentally strong, they have concern over the frequency and intensity of such emergency actions.Beijing increased tariffs selectively and focused on sectors where the impact would signal resolve without triggering runaway instability. The PBOC implemented liquidityâ€‘support measures such as lowering reserve requirement ratios and guiding stateâ€‘owned banks to extend credit in vulnerable export regions. Beijing saw the U.S. episode as a temporary volatility event rather than systemic failure. Chinese experts evaluated the situation and concluded that the Fedâ€™s intervention was a necessary adjustment rather than a reflection of deep structural weakness.The EU and many BRICS members expressed concern over the vulnerabilities exposed by the U.S. crisis. They are reâ€‘evaluating their reliance on the U.S. financial system, with some calling for a more multipolar arrangement in global finance.Trump is seemed as impulsive and hysterical while Xiâ€™s reaction, by contrast, was measured.So, while the Fedâ€™s actions certainly helped to calm the bond market and provided necessary liquidity, they did not amount to a full-blown â€œmonetary easingâ€ package like a rate cut or aggressive quantitative easing program. U.S. policymakers appeared to view the April 9 volatility as a technical market disruption that could be managed with these focused measures.In contrast, Chinese authorities implemented more explicit liquidityâ€‘providing measures. These included lowering the reserve requirement ratios (RRRs) and guiding stateâ€‘owned banks to expand credit in exportâ€‘sensitive sectors. Such measures are textbook examples of monetary easing aimed at supporting domestic demand and stabilizing financial conditions. These moves aim to offset external shocks (like U.S. tariffs and global market volatility) and support domestic demand.Howeverâ€¦Trumpâ€™s strategy marked by (steep tariffs on Chinese imports) directly increases the cost of Chinese goods in the U.S. market. This can hurt export-oriented sectors of the Chinese economy, reducing profits and potentially curbing further investment. When China is relying on monetary easing to stimulate domestic activity, a significant hit to its export revenues can undermine its overall policy effectiveness. Furthermore, aggressive U.S. trade policy may force Chinese companies to accelerate diversification of their supply chains away from the U.S., thereby shortening the window during which monetary easing can generate growth.During periods of global economic retrenchment, countries that excessively eased monetarily without corresponding improvements in trade balances faced capital flight and depreciation pressures. Trump's policies, by intensifying trade friction, may create an environment where Chinaâ€™s easing is insufficient to sustain growth, forcing further aggressive policy measures that could eventually destabilize its financial markets. This would diminish investorsâ€™ confidence in China, and would force China into an even tighter policy cornerâ€”possibly exposing vulnerabilities that it hoped to offset through easing alone.The question is whether Trump dares to take further aggressive steps against his opponent. As mentioned yesterday, the risk/reward doesn't favor him.Back to the initial ideaBut letâ€™s not forget something Iâ€™ve been saying since March:The goal remains a U.S.â€“Russia agreement, which also requires the participation of the globalist elite. The tariffs and all other measures aim, on one hand, to bring the globalist elite to the negotiating table with the U.S. On the other hand, Trump needs to eradicate stagflation from the American economy, which can only be done with drastic stepsâ€”and that requires genuine deflationary fear. This is a dangerous game because, on one hand, foreign investors lose confidence; on the other, the EU is finding new sources for itself with China.Thatâ€™s why Trump, using the double feint we discussed above, is trying to block China, andâ€”if Iâ€™m correctâ€”it seems the bluff is working.Continue reading this post for free, courtesy of Alma.Claim my free postOr purchase a paid subscription.PreviousNext

# Window of Risk, Disbalance - Investigating the story that the market tells

> "Market has become more fragile than it seems" / Asymmetric drawdown risk

**Date:** unknown
**Source:** https://stochvoltrader.substack.com/p/window-of-risk-disbalance-investigating

---

Market analysisWindow of Risk, Disbalance - Investigating the story that the market tells"Market has become more fragile than it seems" / Asymmetric drawdown riskSep 18, 2025âˆ™ Paid472ShareEarlier window of risk callsAfter the April crash my primal window of risk was the May ECB rate decision to mid-May (Trump visit to ME) window. We had a ~97 pts decline but the market didnâ€™t broke my target from where the momentum could have shifted to left-tailish. Later, a 124 pts decline materilaized after Trumpâ€™s visit, but it wasnâ€™t the risk I expected, it was strictly derived from the weekly positioning with an exact target. Same as with the June 16-20 week with its 107 pts decline.The next, and basically my (orignial) major window of risk was the July/August window, post-NFP and FOMC period after tariff effects start to appear in the datas. It was clear since April. I marked it into mid-August. They indeed started to appear in the datas, and we got a 215 pts decline, still didnâ€™t broke my target, that would have triggered the left-tail risk that I kept mentioning. Later, mid-August, another 138 pts decline happened, but still didnâ€™t broke the structure.(I add here that it started to be clear for me during mid-July that the market will remain supported, and I emphatized that my window of risk is shifting towards post-Sept OpEx period)The current window of risk is from post-OpEx Tuesday to October buyback window (as 75% of SPX market cap is in blackout by early October, and funds like to de-risk). Before I jump into the reasons, let me tell youâ€¦â€¦what is the problem?Trumpâ€™s first admin produced a market performance of this kind: And the Covid-crash rebound looked like this: As you can see, the post-Covid rebound wasnâ€™t as quick as it looks first. It produced three more than 9% drops. After the 2018 pullback of 20.21% range the market also experienced 6-7% pullbacks that were higher than proportional compared to the Covid-rebound. In both case, they happened in the following 8 months period before taking out the ATH.Proportionally, it would be like if we would have gotten some 5-6% pullbacks after the current 21.35% April crash before taking off the February all-time-high around December/next-January. And because velocity is the ratio between the path and the time, this is why people say, this rebound is simply just too fast. It feels instable for literally anybody because of its velocity and lack of gap-fillings, and retested structure of liquidity.At the first look, you may think that the momentum is heavily imbalanced. But it is not quite true. The purple line shows us the accumulated imbalances:  As you can see, the time correction ate up a significant % of it, but not all of it. The returns are not that overstretched as they may appear on the price chart. Just for comparsion, the Covid-rebound and the 2021 bull run looked like this: The imbalances were moderated pretty fast and were kept on a relatively low level during 2021 using time correction. The Feb-April selloff did nothing momentum wise but took back all the 2024 imbalances when it reached 5901.88, created a sharp negative disbalance that were also rebalanced when it rebound to 5619.08 What I want to say with all of these, is that the market is not as disbalanced as it may first seems. So, the risk that the different mean reversion algorhytms are meassuring, running on the wrong assumptions (moving averages, gaussian z-scores, exponential growth assumptions or different fair value assumptions) misjudge the trend. If momentum is carefully slowed down, the effect of time correction can mitigate all of these risks coming from assumed disbalanced returns. But, the key takeaway here is that, even knowing that the market has relatively more uncorrected disbalance than it was after past crashes, it is at a level that can be easily managed.The risk doesnâ€™t emerge from momentum, it emerges from something elseâ€¦ SubscribeWhat is the risk? The force that pushed this market even higher in this agressive manner is structural flows.My subs know that day after day, week after week, we keep seeing dealers profiles with negative speed exposures, positive vomma and zomma exposures and pricings that collect the premiums from short sellers.Meanwhile the stock-to-bond correlation is sharply positive on the monthly tenor, SPX/VIX beta is also getting longer since the August sell-off, while investor portfolios keep showing outflow into bonds, cash and other safe-havens.The purpose of the concentrated and agressive negative speed profile is to overwrite the growing SPX skew (net longer vanna) since 2024 June (roughly) on the short-run. What it generates is a short-term positively skewed implied distribution, that generates liquidity usually just right below the spot, and creates a less-resistant but more illiquid paths upward, supported by dealer hedging flows. (This is what I like to call as â€œstunt matsâ€ below the spot)We also keep seeing the constant vomma supply, that does nothing but dampens the tailedness of that implied distribution. Doing this by literally making the volatility to rangebound, not letting it to make sharp moves (it does almost the same to volatility, that gamma does to the momentum, hence its other name: â€œvolgaâ€ that is VOLatility + GAmma). What it takes is locally not letting upside gamma squeezes to materialize in a violent fashion, basically producing a â€œbraking-effectâ€ on any upside moves and short squeezes. Also, to the downside it doesnâ€™t let the vanna to wake up the put hedges on different tenors, so those get mostly closed or let to expire worthless, providing further mechanical buyback flows.Zomma is also shifting to positive as we moving upward, that is a clear sign that we have reached the far right wing of the curve. The system is designed to not let volatility to be suppressed or expanded forever, and zomma is the force that can have an ultimate stopping effect on vol if not anything else. (Again, zomma is capping the volatility by braking the momentum because zomma is the relationship between volatility and gamma, so if vol goes up, the upside gammas start to increase, thus as a result the local dealer hedging flow kicks in and swallows most of the volatility and braking down the momentum. Doesnâ€™t necessary stop it, but brakes it downâ€¦ it is the vol expansion/suppression that it is designed to stop through the momentum.) The other structural flow is generated by the agressive volatility suppression: After volatility mean-reverted sharply and too fast (momentum-wise), it was set to rangebound through the constant vomma supply, currently at 9.76%. VIX was shorted down by 76.17% during the past 5 months, that is literally the fastest decline in its record. The short vol trade is a very crowded trade, bidding up gamma into this expiration. CTAs and Vol Controls and any funds that rely on risk parity, increased their exposure mechanically on this rally, as volatility rangebounded with a slight negative drift. However, the agressive fashion of this vol suppression is crucial, because it made these funds near to their 3-yrs average. And this is a huge positional imbalance, because if volatility catches up towards 13.59% (margin of error is 14.13%), the unwind would be asymmetrical as well, while upside is getting exhausted already, from structural flows perspective. Running codes, CTAs wonâ€™t buy into any 1-2% rallies, but if the SPX falls by 1.5 - 2% they will start to decrease the weight of their allocations, slowly. Especially in NDX, that has the leading weight in this momentum. If NDX falls by only 2.15 - 2.5%, CTAs will be triggered to sell. But anyway, in tech they already reached their max allocation, so they decrease the weight anyway. The momentum is overly concentrated into 0DTE flows and AI momentum, especially after August, accumulating concentrated and excess leverage. So, the disbalance is not emerging directly from the nature of the market momentum, but from the momentum of the volatility reversion and the positioning it generated. Even a small spike that vomma â€œcannot handleâ€ (because vomma has a net inverse relationship with volatility), could trigger a violent drawdown. I give levels belowâ€¦ SubscribeWhat is the story that the market wants to tell us?Market cannot always be frontrunned. During the past months, all I could do is to meassure the risk, derive trigger levels and adjust my longer-term portfolio strategy. The April drawdown was clear as sun, after I decoded what was the meaning behind the February slow selloff.Now, all what we are seeing is that the market is agressively held for any price, pushed further and further up, hunting short squeeze triggers of funds, agressive vol supply, and so onâ€¦ on what? On the narrative of Fed cut that would unlock MMF money to support growth and to manage debt burden. The admin must have built a goldilocks narrative around this economy in order to mask the obvious stagflation and future inflation risk. (Debt burden is way more dangerous.)The story behind the February-April selloff was to bleed-out opponents from the tech sector and to provide discount prices for the Middle-Eastern investors and Trumpâ€™s circle. The market is planned to be reset on a Web3 basis into 2028/30 latest, and until then it will be accelerated, and inflation will be pushed up. I heard from higher government circles and Middle-Eastern investment fund circles that they all plan to trigger a crisis around that period in order to reallocate money into the new economic systemâ€¦ if everything goes along the plan between the USA-RUS and Middle-East, without EU intercepting it. I already elaborated on this in my AI-Bubble post. Okay, but this is the longer-term picture, painting us a prolonged bull market on tech, inflation, gold and yieldsâ€¦But what about now? Who is interested to agressively hold the market up with hightened risk, elevated spot/vol beta, high leverage and very asymmetric positioning? Because it all looks tooâ€¦ directâ€¦Well, I kept emphatizing since April, that this market is not investorâ€™s market but traderâ€™s market. Investors have no confidence on this leg up, they slowly derisking. The April sell-off was also mostly caused by them (weighing machine).All the buy-the-dip flow was speculative buying flow, simply to generate extra money on this leg-up, but also keeping the risk high after some point (the first 80%+ risk signal appeared in July for me). And because of the risk growing, most funds simply scaling out of those positions, that the insiders can accumulate slowly, while they generate enough money for a buyback lower later. 1) funding markets gapping (SOFR > IORB for days, heavy SRF usage), 2) UST market depth collapsing / big bid-ask, 3) primary credit issuance shutting, 4) systematic-strategy de-lever triggers (CTA/vol-control) and 0DTE flows amplifying intraday moves, 5) broad de-risking in flows6) Buyback at discount, gaining more power in those sectorsThis is the scheme. Only thing needed is a trigger. Trump has deals to announce slowly, and AI cap-ex boom, to push some more legs higher and to keep any drawdown under check, sentiment-wise. Mark this trend line, as it is something that matters pretty much: PCE on Sept 26, CPI on Oct 15, or further growing unexpected weakness in labor markets all can be triggers for example for a 6-10% pullback to take back the â€œFed cut - hypeâ€ returns.As the economic management is already known, it would be my very solid and confident buy the dip move for the next 3-5 years. Market analysisZomma & vomma risk as a response to macro, Weekly post (19-23/May)May 18, 2025âˆ™ Paid4436ShareWe have a lot of Fed speakers and PMI (thursday) and VIX expo this coming week. May OpEx was supportive, and for me this still seems topish. Even more.My reversion model shows 77.94% probability for a reversal towards 5518.31 (lvl that stil wouldnâ€™t be trendchanging, but would confirm bullish trend and build the structure for a longer time frame; like monthly), closer to my target of 80%+.
